“Why did you not tell me you were changing? I thought you were unable to on Ibea.”
“Well, you thought wrong. Not for the first time.”
“Fine, but why not a horse, Shiv? We could cover more ground that way.” Adley scratched the fae’s ears, pushing his unease aside.
“Horses don’t have the best sense of smell. A bloodhound is the way today.” He began to circle the room, sniffing deeply. “I need something of the girl’s, something that smells of her.”
Adley went to her bedroom; on the bed was a small, tatty homemade toy lion, well-worn and stained. He had seen her hug it when she cried, and whisper her secrets to it. He couldn’t leave it.
“Will this do?” he asked, holding out the lion.
The dog sniffed excitedly. “Yes, yes, perfect!” With the scent now memorised, he began the track. “This way!” He barked in excitement, his tail wagging.
“Wait! What about the girl?” Adley pointed at Acelle.
The dog’s tail stopped wagging drooped between his legs. “Oh, yes. Of course.”
Adley sheathed his sword and put the toy lion in his pack. He would keep it, in case Sheiva needed a reminder of her scent. He hoisted Acelle into his arms; rigor mortis had come and gone, and she was now limp and ice cold. He tried hard to quell his anger; her death had been needless. He carried her out to the rear of the house and as he began to dig a grave next to those of Glin and Nina Dresne, he thought back to their deaths. They had caught the hack, and the fever had taken them both suddenly. Chaeli’s sorrow when she had returned from the three-day church vigil and found their bodies still haunted him. She had dealt with all the funeral arrangements alone while she waited for Acelle to return from her own vigil. It was then that Adley realised just how much he cared for her. She wasn’t like his previous charges, who took more than they gave. For when Acelle had returned, she had comforted her with a calm façade, then quietly cried herself to sleep. The constant stream of mourners were met with gratitude and open arms, when he knew she wanted nothing more than to curl into a ball and beg the gods for respite from the pain of her loss. She had untapped strength and resolve hiding under an abrasive, sometimes petulant forthrightness, but a compassion and kindness that shone through. The night of the funeral, he had wanted to cradle her to sleep and ease her pain, but could only watch from the shadows.
After several hours of digging, the grave was finally deep enough. Using a bed sheet from the house, he delicately bound Acelle and performed the rites. He plucked a single rose from a bush in the garden, and placed it in the grave with her. Acelle had always loved the roses.
“May the servants of our gods guide you to the light and the earth.
Let them escort you with honour and love,
As the seasons change, so will you,
For this is not the end.
Growing stronger, wiser, and divine,
I will see you again. Blessed be.”
Adley bowed his head and prayed to Daro. He prayed that Acelle had indeed been guided to the Kingdom, where she could find eternal happiness and peace. Sheiva approached the grave. He crouched on his haunches, threw his head back, and let out a haunting howl. When his mourning wail ended, dog and man filled in the grave together. First light burst through the clouds as they finished. Adley was tired, but he knew every moment he rested was a minute more Chaeli was alone. Brushing the mud from his trousers, he stared out at the fields. The fight with the demon may have only lasted moments, but the memory would last forever. Chaeli’s home had been tainted by the Underworld, and she would never find peace here again.
Deep in thought, he wandered to the front of the house, but stopped in his tracks at the sight of two dark-clothed figures emerging from the entrance, their arms laden with the Dresne family’s goods.
“What in the Kingdom’s name are you doing?” he growled, drawing his spine sword once more and striding towards them. The shorter of the two dropped Nina’s jewellery box to the ground and the contents scattered on the cobbles.
“What’s it to you?” the other man sneered, dropping his bag to the floor and pulling out two daggers. He nudged his companion with his elbow and jerked his head at the discarded treasure.
Adley glanced at their wrists. Neither had a band – this would be too simple. He centred his weight on the balls of his feet and allowed his energy to flow.
“You shouldn’t be here, protector, there’s nothing for you. She’s gone.” The larger man chuckled, advancing slowly. He matched Adley in height and build but was clearly several years younger.
“You know who I am, sir – you have me at a disadvantage.” Adley circled the man, who grinned and rolled his shoulders, matching each of the protector’s steps with his own. The man moved slowly, prowling, his gaze locked on Adley’s.
“Andraes. Friends call me Andy. Crusader, surely you recognise me?”
The name startled Adley and he gripped the hilt of his sword tighter. Andraes? Surely not.
“No reply, crusader?”
“You play games with me, commoner. I grow tired.” Adley moved forward and Andraes stepped to his right, lashing out with his dagger. Lurching forward and twisting his body, Adley felt the material on his doublet snag as the blade made contact. Too close. Adley lunged and attacked with a horizontal cut; Andraes blocked it, raising both arms. Blade met blade with a dull clunk and, weapons raised, the men stood face to face. The stench of the Underworld hit the protector and he instinctively drew in his energy to raise a protective shield.
“You left me to die,” snarled Andraes, his face screwed up with fury as he pushed his weight against the sword. 
Adley struggled to maintain his footing, stumbling back and breaking contact with the daggers. “I’m . . . sorry. It wasn’t . . . meant . . . to—”
“Enough false apologies. We all died because you refused to listen.” Andy spat on the ground and raised his dagger. “Your pride and arrogance killed us all. We should have known not to trust you. You were never one of us.”
Adley attacked again with a vertical cut followed in quick succession by three downward swings, but they were blocked easily. Where’s Sheiva? He didn’t want to hear any more, and so spun to face Andraes, who flicked his blond hair from his eyes and laughed, his face just as Adley remembered it. Adley shook his head and tried to concentrate; he couldn’t afford to be distracted my memories.
“I’ve waited many years for this, crusader,” Andraes said, and advanced, his serrated blades glinting. “I’ve dreamt about this, over and over again. It’s the only thing that kept my mind intact in that place . . . the thought of you on the end of my blade.” Adley noted a slight movement in his hands, and Andraes moved to a palm grip, his fingers flat on the ricasso.
Sensing the onslaught Adley attacked, aiming for the man’s left thigh, and at the same time he released a blow of energy to Andraes’s head, temporarily blinding him. The younger man cursed and stumbled, and Adley struck deeply and twisted his blade into the flesh. His blade hit bone, and he pushed deeper until a satisfying crack! pierced the air. Andraes screamed and swiped wildly at Adley’s arm. There was a snarl and the silver fur of Sheiva flashed into view. The changeling bared his teeth and growled, the deep sound rumbling through the morning air. Andraes sagged slightly, blood flowing freely from his thigh – Adley had nicked the artery, a fatal blow.
“You have killed me again, crusader. Did you not already have enough of my blood on your hands?” The hate in his voice burned as he collapsed to the ground. “Finish me, Adley! Or do you lack the courage?”
Adley looked round for the second man, but he was gone.
“Sheiva, there is another – find him!”
While the dog searched, Adley knelt next to the dying man.
“How, Andraes? How did you come back?”
“Desperation.”
“Desperation?”
“We all do desperate things for love, crusader. My desperation overrode my sense. The price was worth it.” He coughed and Adley touched his pale, clammy forehead. All was silent, not even the birds sang.
“What price?”
“My soul. My prince has my soul and calls upon me when needed.”
“Your soul?” Adley whispered.
“She was worth it, Adley. ”
Adley tried to pray for the fallen man, but Andraes grabbed his wrist. “You can’t pray for me now. There is only one place I will go.” His voice cracked and grew quieter. “I’ll see you again, crusader. I’ll have . . . my . . . vengeance.”
“Why were you here?”
“For . . . his daughter, she’s so like him, but . . . she was gone.” The words gave way to more coughing. Andraes closed his eyes, and the shallow rise and fall of his chest became erratic. Then there was silence.
Adley slowly stood and picked up his sword. Already the body of Andraes was disappearing, recalled to his master. Guilt rose in Adley, but he swallowed his eternal shame. He cleaned his sword and replaced it, then scooped Nina’s jewellery into the box and put it back inside the house.
The bloodhound in the doorway eyeing him curiously. “He’s gone, Adders. I can’t track him. What was that all about, anyway?”
“Where were you?” Adley’s voice was accusatory, but the changeling stood his ground.
“Tracking. I sensed something in the woods. Who were they?”
“Looters.”
“Looters who knew you.”
Adley shook his head. “You are mistaken. I’ll check the rest of the house, you check outside.” The dog turned and left in silence. Adley ensured all the doors and windows were bolted closed, then joined him.
“Let’s go,” he said at last, pushing the meeting with Andraes to the back of his mind. He was glad Daro had sent Shieva. It was good to have a companion.
The dog sniffed the ground in an arc. “This way!”
Together they walked through the copse, dog leading, man following.
Chapter Three
A week had passed since Chaeli arrived at Anya’s. Uncomfortable with merely sitting around, Chaeli insisted on cleaning and cooking whenever she could. When packing blankets away under the bed, she found a collection of tightly-wrapped scrolls. Stroking the delicate parchment, she listened for the creak of footfalls coming up the stairs before she opened one and scanned the contents: it was the retelling of the Tale of the Two Brothers.
She opened another scroll, and another. All were holy scriptures. Reaching further under the bed, she pulled out a wooden box full of leather-bound and handwritten books on the Eternal Kingdom, after asking for Anya’s permission, she spent hours reading whilst the older woman saw to patients. Several times she helped Anya when asked, but was more of a hindrance than anything. Anya never complained, but even she winced as Chaeli managed to tie a bandage so tightly that a young man’s fingers started to turn blue. He, fortunately, believed this to be some sort of healing magic.
The evenings were spent together; Nathan – who disappeared during the day to return with scrapes on his hands and a guarded expression that told Chaeli not to ask questions – taught her the card game of molyona, and Anya delighted in embarrassing Nathan with stories of his childhood. Now and then a darkness would come into the room as they contemplated the gap between childhood innocence and their subsequent stories: the aching awareness of what had been stolen away from them all, and what had been given willingly.
Chaeli’s thigh was immeasurably better. The skin had already scabbed over, and she could walk and run without pain. Each night she prayed to Elek, and he bestowed his blessing upon her. His touch would stroke along her skin, soothing the pain in both her wound and her soul, gently consoling her.
With no clothes other than those Nathan had found her in, she had tried to sew the trousers, however when Anya found her patching them up, she tutted and sighed, declaring that she needed new clothes for the city. In the interim, and with a little persuasion from his sister, Nathan gave her a pair of his own trousers to wear. She tried them on, much to the amusement of her hosts, for they were huge and slipped from her waist constantly.
After that, Nathan brought her dresses, for which she was grateful, even though they were gaudy and unfashionable; they proved comfortable and flattering, and that was good enough. Chaeli was used to trousers – her mother had spent years trying to cajole and persuade her into dresses but she steadfastly refused. The village seamstress had been unaccustomed to designing clothes for women of Chaeli’s height, and the one time she had made her a frock it fitted badly across the chest and was far too short. She hated wearing dresses, much preferring the freedom trousers gave her, though it had pained her mother to see her dressed that way. “It’s like I have two sons!’” she would say with a sigh and a shake of her head.
When Chaeli woke on her sixth day it was still dark, but Nathan was gone; lately he had taken to leaving earlier and returning later each day.
“Don’t worry, Chaeli, he has business,” Anya had said when Chaeli asked his whereabouts.
But his serpents had burned their own mark on her, and she found herself wanting to know more of him and his time in Trithia. She wanted to believe Nathan truly had severed all ties with the Underworld, but she knew too little about him to be sure, and each time she tried to ask, the scars on Anya’s face silenced her.
After the lowsun prayers, she found it difficult to concentrate on the archaic language and lay on the bed reading. The demon had mentioned scriptures when she had boasted to him her knowledge, and he had smiled at her knowingly. She pictured the black-cloaked figure who disappeared when the man entered. She couldn’t remember seeing the face, but she did remember white hands – it had white hands, she was certain. It had taken her blood, but what did the demon want with that? And why hadn’t it taken Acelle’s? It was said that blood rituals were performed in the Underworld, and accusations of such practices were often levelled against minorities who aroused distrust. She remembered the stories at her small village school of how souls were captured and controlled through blood. Surely they were nothing more than childish whispers? She remembered then the school mistress chastising another student with her switch when they had dared tease and scare the miller’s twin daughters with such a story.
Restless on the bed, she moved to the table and began to read another dry, old and very dull scroll. The fading ink and rough handwriting made it difficult to understand, but just as she opened her mouth to call Anya for help, the tinkling of the medicinia door distracted her.
“Can I help you?” There was something in Anya’s voice that prickled Chaeli, and so she backed away quietly, to slide under Anya’s bed. She stared through the metal grate in the wall down into the main room. Anya stood by her shelves, a towel in her hands and her headscarf covering most her face. Flitting her glance across the room, Chaeli took a sharp intake of breath; four King’s Guard stood by the door, the red of their jackets out of place in the white room.
“By the order of King Stirm, we’re here to ask some questions, ma’am. Do you mind if we sit?” The older, and obvious commander of the men didn’t wait for a reply and instead sat at the bench, gesturing for Anya to sit opposite.
“I’ll stand, thank you, I have things to do. Ask your questions.” She turned her back on them and fiddled with bottles, organising their labels so they faced outwards. The three soldiers by the door were silent, which, for Chaeli, made them more dangerous.
“Ma’am, for the record, what is your full name?”
Turning the last bottle around slowly and staring at the label for long moment, Anya turned to him and lowered her scarf, tucking her hair behind her ears. The soldiers at the door stepped back and one clumsily hit the bell. Only the tinkling of metal on metal filled the air as they took in her mutilated face.
Holding her breath to silence the pounding in her ears, Chaeli strained to hear the trio whisper: “It’s the traitor’s sister . . . the forbidden . . . by the gods, look at her!”
The guard at the table stood and averted his gaze, his distaste clear. “We shall take our leave.”
If their reactions upset Anya, she didn’t let it show. “Sir, may I ask why you came here?”
“Ma’am, please, I apologise for the intrusion. We shall be leaving.” He nodded to his men, who filed out into the street. “We shan’t trouble you again.” As he too stepped outside, he shot one final look around the room, his glance lingering on the stairs for a second. “Do you live alone?”
“You know I do,” was her reply, and with one final nod, he left. Anya locked the door and turned back to the shelves, her face impassive.
Chaeli wriggled out and dusted herself down, then sat back at the table and stared at the parchment. Eventually her eyes started to glaze over; she felt trapped in the quarters after so many days. She needed fresh air, and contact with other people.
After an hour of staring at the same words, she decided to visit the market. A mixture of dread, hope, despair and memories had overwhelmed her until the tears fell and she needed a distraction.
Perhaps she would surprise Nathan and Anya with some special dish – the shopping and preparation would help keep her mind calm, and maybe she could begin to repay the debt she owed them both. She told Anya she was going for a walk to get some supplies for the kitchen, and so Anya dutifully handed over a pouch of coins without a word. This made Chaeli acutely aware that she was out of place, and as much as she wanted it to be, this wasn’t her home. Now she was forced to borrow money from them and would have to pay them back.
Stepping outside, she breathed in deep and allowed the smells of the city to entice her: mead, blood, meat, fruits, spices, perfume. The delicate aroma of Algarian oranges wafted through the air, making her smile – her father had bought her mother some of the expensive foreign perfume one birthday, and Chaeli couldn’t recall her mother ever being so delighted as she has been when she opened that gift.
This was the first time Chaeli had left Anya’s dwelling alone, and she drank in the sights. There were more buildings than she could count, the light-grey stone dull and monotonous. But each shopkeeper had personalised his space with coloured glass, calligraphy, or brightly coloured cloth. Mischlan cloth – the most sought after silk and gauze in all of the Seven Realms, delicately spun from the larvae of the Mischlan Desert fire ants – was cooling in the heat and warming in the cold, but very expensive. Here, however, many traders displayed the cloth.
As she wandered around, Chaeli spied two other healers, their price lists boldly displayed. Chaeli almost choked: they charged double and sometimes triple Anya’s rates.
The walking grew easier with each step, and Chaeli revelled in the feeling of renewal. She retraced her steps to the larger market by the entrance to the castle, the hustle and bustle of the busy square exhilarated her, and she easily melted in the crowd.
This was nothing like her village; even the haggling was more exciting. At a fresh food stall she spied a familiar pale yellow vegetable buried under a pile of turnips. Her mouth watered. Cret’cha! She hadn’t eaten the spicy vegetable for months, and it was a rare find.
“How much for the cret’cha?” she called over the cries of other traders.
“Three silver.”
“Pull the other one,” she shouted back, laughing. “Three silver for one cret’cha? Have the decency to wear a mask if you intend to rob me!”
The trader roared with laughter and wagged a finger at her. “For you, two silver and a kiss!”
“How about I give you four coppers and we’ll shake on it?”
“A silver and five coppers! Cret’cha is out of season at the moment and I’m the only trader in Trithia with a supply! Feel free to try elsewhere, but I’ll not drop a copper below.”
“Agreed, but only if you throw in a handful of those ratatta herbs and six good sized ‘tatoes.”
The trader chuckled and nodded. “Aye, but you drive a hard bargain.”
She handed over the coin, excited at the thought of surprising her hosts. The bartering had revived her further, bringing a small part of her lost self back. Weaving through the stalls, she stopped to admire the jewellery – a weakness of hers. Many summer nights had been spent with Ven, the artisan who lived across the fields. She was fascinated by his skill, and when her parents died she found comfort in hard stones and cold metal, twisting and shaping them into unusual trinkets. Ven had often enthused over her natural talent, but she had dismissed his words as flattery; though his offer of an apprenticeship had arrived the morning the demon attacked. She hadn’t the time to tell Acelle the news. Not before what had happened.
The jeweller’s stall had felt mats spread on the tables, each laden with sparkling stones and precious metals. Chaeli selected a delicate filigree ring in silver and yellow gold, and slipped it onto her finger. Whoever had crafted this piece had skill. Reluctantly, she took it off again and held it in her palm, gazing at it admiringly.
“That would look well on a beautiful woman such as yourself,” an amused voice remarked from behind her. “Though it would be flattered by the association.” Chaeli turned to face the man who had spoken. He was of a similar height to her, with deep moss-green eyes, messy dark hair and an open smile. She stepped back. Why was he staring at her so? Did she have something in her hair? Running her hands over her head, she hesitantly returned his smile and placed the ring back on the mat, nodding to the merchant, who stared at her suspiciously.
The young man leant forward. “I don’t mean to offend you.”
“No offence taken.” She noted the expensive leather shoes in the traditional Algary style. His coat was stylish and understated, but undoubtedly had cost a small fortune, and he wore a huge solid gold signet ring. Thinking back to her school lessons on civility and courtesy, she recognised the symbol as being one of the royal houses of Algary – but her ignorance bested her again when she couldn’t identify it. She could almost hear the voice of the school mistress. “Why does it not surprise me, Chaeli? Never listening, always dreaming. How do you ever expect to rise above the status of trader if you can’t identify the regalia of your betters?”
“My name is Kee Dala,” he said, “and after looking into your eyes, I am forever at your service.” He bowed and reached towards Chaeli, gently taking her hand and kissing it as though she were royalty and he a commoner. She turned scarlet and pulled her hand back more roughly than she intended. “May I have the pleasure of your name? Or will I have to beg?”
“Chaeli Dresne,” she replied, then immediately regretted her openness.
“Chaeli! What an enchanting name, I shall arrange for the chief balladeer of Algary to sing of your eyes and the way they have bewitched me.”
She couldn’t stop her cheeks from flushing and found herself unnerved, unable to respond coherently. She knew she looked dowdy and plain in his company, dressed in a simple shift dress paired with her cumbersome boots. “I…have to go. I’m sorry I can’t stay and talk, I’m running late. Time, you know. Going.”
She started to leave, but her admirer feigned shock and hurt. “My charms must be failing! Othos, help me!” he called to the God of Song, spreading his arms like the most hammy of balladeers. She was worried now; perhaps she had misunderstood, perhaps this man was unwell. But all she could see was a young man with a full and simple smile and an honest face. She glanced around for any signs of deceit or ambush, or a suggestion that there was some audience watching, but she found none that explained his interest in her.
“I’m really sorry, but I must leave, thank you, it has been very . . . entertaining.”
“Alas, I know when my time is up, and when I have been denied. Oh, cruel Penella!” he cried, gripping his chest. He bowed deeply. “Farewell, Chaeli Dresne, may Drenic bless your path and Elek shower you with good fortune.”
Turning with an attempt at some dignity, she smiled despite herself. Hurriedly she began walking away. She could feel his eyes still on her and she looked back at him one last time. As she did, he turned away and stared pointedly at the jewellery.
She took her time walking back to Anya’s. The sun was setting and the cool air passed over her, caressing and calming. This place was so full of variety and life. She hoped she could stay. She considered asking Anya if she could lodge with her, perhaps even become her pupil, create a new life, a new Chaeli – anything to forget the horrors and block out the lies of that demon. 
As she entered the house, the strong aroma of cleaning balms stung her eyes again. Anya greeted her briskly and continued to scrub the floor.
“I’ve got something special for supper,” said Chaeli. “Do not disturb me and I shall create a wonderful soup that will tantalise your taste buds.”
Anya laughed and agreed to leave her alone. Chaeli bounded up the stairs to the kitchen and busied herself with dinner preparations. She needed something familiar, something comforting.
*
“Why does Father insist on keeping us in this god forsaken city?” complained Fyn. “I miss home . . . and the pretty women.”
Dal looked up from his letters. “Ah, dear brother, our father trusted me with his reasoning but not you.” He tapped his nose. “I am sworn to secrecy.”
Fyn threw himself onto the sofa. “Why does Father never charge me with one of his missions? I long for action and excitement. It’s not fair.”
“He probably doesn’t trust you.” Dal raised a hand before the youth could object. He was in no mood for an argument. “No, trust is perhaps the wrong word. Father needs someone older to handle his affairs. Be grateful I’m his lapdog and not you. I yearn to chase pretty women instead of pompous diplomats, and drown each night in wine instead of ink.”
“I just don’t know why Father doesn’t even ask me. We’re not so different, you and me.”
Dal stopped writing and raised a quizzical eyebrow. “Fyn, you and I are exceedingly different in height, age, colouring, and blood – we even have different mothers.”
“We all have different mothers, but that doesn’t stop Father sending Nyla on missions, and she’s a woman!”
Dal sighed. “Enough, brother, your time will come. Father knows us well and plays to our strengths. Nyla’s happen to be on her back, and mine involve my handsome looks, wit, and charm.” He waved his hand airily.
“Again, Dal? Run through that again?” Fyn looked at him with an expression of mock incredulity.
“This game we play involves such subterfuge, I fear you are not cut out for it.”
“Perhaps you’re right, Dal. I am but a simple man with simple needs, and right now those needs cry for attention.” Fyn stood and straightened his tunic. “Red is not my colour. Gods curse this attachment to Stirm’s guard. I count down each day until I can return home. One hundred and thirty two days left.” He walked to the door. As he opened it a waft of putrefying fish filled the air. “Gods, man! Why do you insist on keeping lodgings in this part of the city? There are plenty of apartments in the castle. I could arrange for you to be housed somewhere more fitting within a day.”
“I like the city, and besides, some of my business requires me to be away from the eyes and ears of the King’s Guard. I’d feel stifled in that palace and, gods forbid, Stirm might even demand my attendance. I wouldn’t be able to keep my tongue – in more ways than one – and then poor Father would have one less son to command.”
“It would also mean I would move one rung up the line of succession,” said Fyn, his eyes narrowing. “Perhaps I should suggest it to the captain.”
“Be gone, brother before this becomes a different kind of fencing,” said Dal with a laugh, though feeling none of the humour he displayed.
Fyn smiled, calling back as he left the room: “Another route to my advancement!”
Dal stretched lazily and savoured the silence. Whilst he enjoyed the visits from his younger brother, the boy could talk for the whole of Algary, and then some. Dal enjoyed silence and his own company – the thought of living and sleeping in the royal apartments was his own vision of hell. The constant socialising, the paralysing politeness and political ramblings would coarsen and suffocate his mind. He looked around the sparse room; no, he would stay here in the shadows conducting his father’s business in a degree of secrecy. With quick links to the main market, the castle, and most importantly an exit from the city, he was ideally placed. And the rent and wine were cheap.
He returned to his writing – he had four reports to send his father and three copies of each to send his siblings. Sometimes Dal resented his role, and other times he revelled in it. Today it was the former. After several hours of writing, and an entire bottle of some strong Sheeman wine, his hand cramped, his head hurt, and he longed for fresh air. He carefully sealed each letter with his ring, then threw his jacket on.
Outside, a cacophony of noise filled the air; for all of Stirm’s distractions no one could fault the sheer exuberance of his city. The greatest problem was the Sink, and yet Dal was at home in Trithia, more so even than the courts of Algary. He could be his own man here without the constant scrutiny of his family and the equally constant criticism, open and tacit. He could disappear into the streets here, and no one would know who he was. He could hide his fears and insecurities in a jug of wine or mead and allow the heavy feeling of inadequacy to melt away with each gulp.
He made his way quickly through the streets, enjoying the ever-changing scenes and the interaction of the citizens. Approaching the market, he wandered around aimlessly absorbing snippets of conversation and using the time as an excuse to sober himself:
“Milket, no! Heard old Tibbet got caught over the side again” . . . “taxes going up again, apparently His Majesty wants another temple for Kry’lla” . . . “I ‘eard the Prince of House Montareon is ‘ere. Prince Kee Dala ‘imself, hiding in Trithia . . .”
Dal smothered a smile and duly hid. He kept his head low, continuing to listen to the traders and civilians gossip as he weaved through the crowds.
“Aye, I saw the chariots on t’other side of the river, Mischlans” . . . “It was DeVaine, I swear it, I’d recognise him anywhere. Bet you know why ‘e’s ‘ere.”
So the old captain was back and in hiding! Interesting. That was something he would have to send to his father, earn some longed-for and overdue praise. As he followed the source of the information, a woman caught his eye. He watched her as she haggled with a merchant, and was bewitched. She began to laugh, and he caught himself staring at her mouth and neck and then, almost without his will, he started after her, following her through the market. His breath caught and his heart quickened. Never had a woman raised such a reaction in him away from the bedsheets, and yet, though pleasant on the eye, she was rather plain. When she stopped, he stopped too. Her eyes ran covetously over some jewellery, and instantly he approached her.
What am I doing? Dal suddenly reminded himself of his father’s court fools. He spoke, then immediately regretted it, thinking of the bottle that now lay empty in his apartments. He was nervous, and tried to be clever, then wished the ground would swallow him up. Still, she had smiled at him in response, though it was a guarded, un-encouraging smile. He adored her eyes, their unusual shade of blue, a blue which surely had never been seen before, never been matched before. They seemed to stare right to his core and strip him bare.
Like I should do to her . . .
Wait, what was he thinking? Damned wine! He caught her looking him over from top to toe and he became even more self-conscious. The weight of his house ring was a constant reminder of his station and responsibilities: she saw the ring but looked away. This apparent indifference intensified the thrill of her presence. He kissed her hand and begged her name.
“Chaeli Dresne,” she replied and pulled her hand away. Before he could ask, she bade him farewell and left. He watched her walk away. She turned back for a moment, and he pretended to admire the rings she had desired. “How much for the filigree one with the gold inlay?” he asked, idly playing with the silver band on his left wrist and trying to look sideways at her. But she was gone.
*
“It’s the first time the Games have been held in Trithia for nearly one hundred years, Nathan. We’re not missing them because you don’t think it’s safe. Gods, you’re one to talk.” There was a pause. Chaeli slid down to the grate and watched as Anya scribbled in her ledger for the tax collector, sitting at her workbench, head bent and back hunched. Nathan leaned against the shelves with his arms folded, frowning at her. He sported a fresh bruise on his cheekbone and grazes to his knuckles.
“What if people see? They’ll want to know who she is and why she’s with you. I told you, Anya, people are talking. We can’t have attention drawn here, not again.”
“Am I not to have friends? I am forbidden to men, Nathan, not women.”
“I’m sure Stirm won’t care for the difference,” he replied drily. “The next stage of the Games are held in Lindor, perhaps you could—”
“I told you, I’m not leaving.” Anya was angry; her voice wavered and Chaeli involuntarily tensed.
“Why are we having this discussion? She’s not going.”
“Who are you to decide?” Anya stood and poked her brother in the chest with her pen. “This is my home, my business, and the decision is Chaeli’s.”
“Chaeli is my responsibility.”
Chaeli opened her mouth to reply, then snapped it shut. She wanted to say she was no one’s responsibility, but the truth of those words cut too deep, and instead the thought of someone caring for her warmed her. She stifled a sneeze, and the floorboards creaked as she jumped, causing Nathan to stare straight at her through the grate. Without speaking, he turned and left the medicinia, the small bell tinkling as he slammed the door shut.
“You might as well come down,” called Anya.
Guilt stabbed at Chaeli as descended, and tried to apologise, but Anya held up a hand to silence her.
“Don’t worry about it. He’s a stubborn ox and needs to walk it off.” Filing away her books in a drawer, she turned back. “The Principal Games are on tonight and tomorrow in the city. Algarian knife dancing, Sheeman archers, Porton defence form, and Trithian fist-fighting in the docks.”
A thrum of excitement hummed through Chaeli. The underground competition between four of the realms, old as time itself and held every ten years, was always an exciting event. The prize, gifted to the greatest fighter who scored the highest in all events, was a golden chainmail sash handed from winner to winner believed to have once been part of a dragon’s armour in the great war.
“Trithia is hosting an event this year.”
“Which event?”
“Fist fighting, of course.” Pushing a piece of folded parchment across the bench to her, Anya grinned. “Two nights, no holds barred, all with an invite welcome, anonymity god-sworn.”
“I’ve never seen the Games,” Chaeli murmured wistfully.
“Neither have I, not properly at least. I was a child last time.”
“And this is our invite?” Tapping the parchment, Chaeli raised an eyebrow at the older woman who nodded. “And are we going?”
“Yes, I believe we are.”
“Dinner can wait for another night, then,” Chaeli replied, and both women grinned at each other.

An hour later, dressed in her too-large dress and boots, a loosely knitted shawl cinched around her waist as a belt, Chaeli followed Anya through the streets to the wharves. The smell of putrid fish filled the air, causing the younger girl to gag every so often.
“It gets better, I promise,” said Anya, lifting a flap of material and ushering her into a tin-roofed building.
As Chaeli’s eyes adjusted to the dark, a mountain of a man moved to stand almost immediately in front of them. His gold earrings gleaming in the black. Silent as the dark and twice as frightening, he held out his swarthy hands. Dropping the parchment invite in one, and two gold pieces in the other, Anya grabbed Chaeli’s hand and pulled her close. The giant stepped to the side and pushed open the heavy metal door, revealing lantern-lit steps leading down.
“Where does this go?” Chaeli whispered, her voice startlingly loud.
“A sanctuary of sorts. You’ll see.”
Chaeli found herself staring open-mouthed at the stories etched into the hard stone of the walls. Dragons and fae, demons and men, arm in arm they marched towards a triptych inscribed with the vows of spirit and time, the foundations of life on Ibea. The deeper they walked, the louder the music and shouts from below grew. Chaeli could smell boar and apples, and her stomach sang happily to her, sounding more like a growl to the outside world.
The steps ended and the room opened up to a huge subterranean space filled with people, food, music and dancing. There were stands of sweetbreads and sugarlicks, and drums with thick and juicy slices of boar and fresh bread offered freely.
Dear gods, she thought. I could grow fat and happy here.
“Chaeli, come,” called Anya, beckoning her through the throng to a roped ring in the middle of the room. “We need to get a good spot.”
Anya’s scarred cheeks were flushed with excitement, her eyes dancing. No one paid any attention to her broken beauty, however, and the ability to breathe easily without fear of detection lifted the heavy weight she carried. Chaeli could see that Anya stood taller, glowed brighter, and had shed the look of sadness she usually carried. Unsure exactly what to do, Chaeli found herself watching the people who weaved and bobbed through the crowds. There were sharp, short shouts, and jeers, and cackling retorts that cut above the hum of conversation. Two soldiers were heckling a group of Algarian traders. It seemed good-natured enough, but before she could join in the banter and betting, something prickled at the skin on the back of her neck. Turning, she caught a glimpse of a pale-skinned woman staring directly at her: one eye, milky and dead, the other a piercing green. Raising a finger to her lips, the strange woman indicated her silence and nodded towards the ring. Chaeli glanced at the empty fighting space and frowned with confusion. When she looked back, the woman was gone. Surely she hadn’t disappeared? Craning her neck, Chaeli searched for her, but the crowds closed in tighter now and obscured her vision with their bodies.
“Chaeli, are you all right?” asked Anya, watching her with a frown.
“I’m fine. Would you like something to drink?” She dragged her gaze down to the small woman and forced a smile.
Anya smiled. “Mead, thank you.”
Chaeli nodded, then squeezed herself through the crowd, hunting for the strange woman. Unable to understand why, she knew she needed to talk to her. For once, she thanked the gods for her height. Standing shoulder to shoulder with the men, she was able to navigate a path to the food stalls with ease. At the end of a long line and waiting for drink, she hunted for the pale woman again, but just as she thought she saw a glimpse of white-blonde, the musicians put bows to strings and filled the room with music. Momentarily distracted, Chaeli laughed and clapped as acrobats flipped and turned from the corners of the room towards the ring. The acrobats were from Agassia, she was sure. Small and lithe, with blue soles and palms, they lived on an island miles from anywhere but Porton. Fascinated, she failed to notice the long, thick, red rope drop down from the ceiling, and the rope weavers that followed. Shrieking as two of them dropped a garland of flowers around her neck, the crowd roared in laughter and stamped their feet in approval as the scantily clad women turned and twisted on the rope, touching and tantalizing all. The room rumbled and Chaeli reddened at the intimacy displayed to and from the weavers. Tempering her breathing, she forced a smile and admired the flowers. Tiny rainbow buds and white lilies plaited together into the shape of runes: prosperity, luck, joy and . . . the last one couldn’t be right. Blood? Confused, she grabbed two tankards of mead and wandered back through the crowd to Anya.
On her return, she almost dropped the mugs at the sight of Anya laughing and chatting with a tall reedy-looking man. She had never seen her so carefree.
“Chaeli, this is . . . Erik?” She raised an eyebrow at the man, who nodded.
“Erik will do.” He clasped Chaeli’s forearm in greeting, and she immediately noted the band around his wrist: mathematics, obedience, and song. “I’m a tax collector for the guild in the southern quarter.” Sharing a secret smile with Anya, he added, “And I sing at the Golden Trout once a week.”
“Erik’s got three trens on Merlon Derrick winning the first round. I’ve just doubled my bet in favour of his competitor.”
Chaeli remained quiet, letting Anya and Erik share a laugh. The music petered away, and the dancers filed out. Then, a new sound flared up, a drum, heavy and deep with a slow beat. Soon the crowd was clapping in rhythm. Chaeli downed her mead, and Erik immediately handed her another; soon the warmth of the air, and of the alcohol, had her swaying and clapping in time with the others. The richest of the observers sat on a raised dais, their demeanour and clothes betraying their high born status.
“My lords, my ladies,” cried a preternaturally loud voice. Standing on tiptoes, Chaeli spied a small man balancing upon a stool in the centre of the ring. He radiated a presence that screamed of power. Bowing to the nobility present, he turned back to the crowds. “Sirs and madams,”—he winked at the prostitutes who flashed leg and chest at him, and their customers for the evening, who doffed their caps good-naturedly—“welcome all to the first stage of the Principal Games.”
He hopped down from the stool and dodged out of sight; the crowd went silent as two fighters appeared from the shadows. Both wore leather overvests upon bare chests, with runes of protection, luck, and power inked on their visible skin with ash. As they raised their hands and started to walk around the ring, the people exploded into applause again, Chaeli screaming with them, and Anya jumping up and down.
“Chaeli, look,” Anya said, pointing to the second fighter, who circled the audience. Chaeli dropped her mead, splashing her legs and feet.
“Nathan?” Confused, she stared at her friend, who laughed in delight.
“It’s why he’s back. The Games are god-sworn and protected. He can’t be touched here.” Her eyes danced with amusement. “I’m sorry I didn’t tell you, but I wanted to see that look on your face. He was – he is one of the best fighters in the realm. This is his only chance.”
Chaeli studied Nathan, thinking of their meeting, the bruise and grazes, the days and nights away from the surgery. It all fitted now. The arms that had held her so tenderly just a few nights ago were now covered in oil, ready to strike out at his opponent. His jaw tensed when he saw her in the crowds, a look of apprehension and sadness in his eyes. She looked away and down at the dusty ground, not fully understanding why she couldn’t hold his gaze.
“Merlon Derrick, a sword of Porton, fights Nathan DeVaine, former Captain of the King’s Guard of Trithia.”
The crowd roared when the announcer lifted Nathan’s arm, and Chaeli jolted with surprise. An outcast and dishonoured, yet here he was a beloved champion. He shot a look to Anya, and his little sister screamed in excitement while Erik whistled and yelled his support for the huge bald Porton.
“There are five rounds, each lasting one complete twist of the dragon,” shouted the announcer. “The winner will be decided based on points scored.” He pointed to the large brass dragon the wall. “However, should one of you land a sleeping blow, then you automatically win.”
“A sleeping blow?” shouted Chaeli at Anya, shrugging in confusion.
“Knock-out. If Nathan can knock out the Porton, he wins.”
Staring at the monster of a man, Chaeli wasn’t as confident as Anya sounded. He was huge and corded with muscle. Each one of his arms was larger than her waist, and her waist was not as slim as most girls her age. Before she could air her thoughts, the announcer stood on his stool by the dragon and spun it one complete turn, holding the tail in place.
“Fighters, ready?”
Both men moved to the centre of the ring and nodded. Chaeli shoved through the observers to a place at ringside. The ropes were damp and smelt acrid and bitter.
“Get back, miss,” shouted a guard, pushing her into two men. She opened her mouth to curse the guard, but before she could speak, he produced a lit torch that ignited with the ropes with barely a touch. The fire raced along the rope and lit up the entire ring. Shadows danced on the faces of the opponents.
“The dragon roars. Fight!” cried the announcer, releasing the dragon’s tail timer.
Apologising to the men, Chaeli watched as Nathan lashed forward and jabbed the Porton to the face. There was a dull thud as his knuckles made contact with bone, whipping his opponent’s head back. The Porton staggered, his guard raised, and Nathan punched again, but this time it was blocked easily and with such force that Chaeli winced. He landed a blow to Nathan’s ribs and the crowd groaned. Nathan sidestepped around the ring until Chaeli could see his face. His brow knitted together and there was darkness in his eyes. Through the belts of his armour, the snakes moved and licked their way around his chest and neck. Bile burned in her throat and she turned away from the ring, pushing and shoving her way to more open space, all excitement drained away. He fought with power and strength of the Underworld – whether he wanted its influence or not, it was there.
The crowd continued to shout and groan, yell and cry, and eventually a loud buzzer declared the end of the first round. Nathan stood in the corner, drinking water and scanning the crowds in desperation, his gaze finally resting on Chaeli. He shook his head, and it was as though he knew her thoughts, but under the heat of his stare, it suddenly meant very little. The announcer shouted again and the second round started, but with her back against the wall, Chaeli saw and heard little of the fight. Instead, flashes of the demon and Acelle’s body seared through her mind. Her throat started to close and her lungs ached. Bending over she breathed in deeply, gulping at air with a rabid hunger.
“Are you quite all right?” a cool voice asked. She twisted and glanced up, and saw cold ice blue eyes staring down at her. She noted the red of his soldier’s tunic hidden under a brown cloak. The gold thread and piping warned her that he was of military rank.
“I’m fine, thank you.” She straightened, standing taller than he, but he carried himself with such authority and power that their height difference was negligible. Looking her up and down, he nodded just once and then stared at the ring.
“DeVaine fights well. There’s passion in his movements.”
“I suppose so.” She tried to sound enthused, but her voice was dull and low.
“Are you not a fan?” He didn’t look at her, but placed his hands behind his back, the gap in the cloak displaying his daggers in the black leather sheaths.
“I’ve not seen anything like this before.”
The stranger fell silent and Chaeli found herself drawn to him as he watched Nathan. His look was predatory, his eyes following each movement the fighters made.
“DeVaine has this fight, or he should do. I have seven gold pieces on him.” He spoke calmly, and without conceit.
Seven gold pieces? That was more than Chaeli’s father had earned in a year. Transfixed on the fight, the stranger didn’t notice when she edged away and rejoined Anya.
“Round three and Nathan’s two rounds up!” Anya gabbled, placing a hand on Chaeli’s arm. “Oh! Are you all right? You look so pale.”
“I’m fine.”
“Do you want to go?” Anya asked, flitting her gaze to the ring where the announcer declared the start of the next round.
“No, no, I promise, it’s fine.” Smiling weakly, Chaeli forced herself to watch the fight as Nathan pummelled the ribs and kidneys of his opponent. The skin stretched and rippled under the power of his blows. Finally, he maneuvered himself into position and caught the top ribs of the Porton’s left side, driving his full weight into the larger man’s chest. Nathan braced for a return blow, the muscles in his shoulders tensing, but none came.
Merlon Derrick screeched, bending over, gripping his ribs. Nathan growled and punched his opponent in the face. Derrick hit the dusty floor hard, spitting up blood. The crowd howled as Nathan rained down blow after blow from all sides, blasting the breath from the man’s body and rattling his bones. Before long, he was limp and the countdown started. Chaeli found herself holding her breath, both in anticipation and in fear.
“We have a winner!” the announcer cried, stepping over the Porton and throwing his small arms in the air, then flapping them at Nathan. “Nathan DeVaine!”
Anya darted through the crowds, small and lithe, and jumped through the now smoking ropes to hug her brother. Alone on the sidelines, Chaeli hugged herself and watched as several of the nobles made their way down from their stand to shake his hand. He greeted them as though they were old friends, and with a wry grin, Chaeli realised that most of them would be. And, it seemed, old lovers too; an elaborately dressed noblewoman leaned in close and whispered in his ear, her curled hair momentarily sticking to his sweat covered face. He nodded and her tongue flicked out to lick the skin by his ear as she tucked a small piece of paper into his armour. Nathan’s eyes followed her as she sashayed away across the ring with her husband, the low cut of her dress showing a vast expanse of tattooed skin: a bear and a lizard fidgeted and fought each other irritably and crawled across her shoulders.
The excitement died away, and Chaeli found herself questioning the high entry price and hype of the event – one gold piece for just a handful of time and show? It didn’t seem right. She crossed to the ring and waited for Anya and Nathan to finish their conversation with the announcer and, Chaeli presumed, a patron or lord, for he handed Nathan a pouch of money and slapped him on the shoulder, thanking him for the fight and urging him towards the next round.
Leaving Anya with the others, Nathan faced Chaeli, the bruise on his cheek a vivid purple now.
“You look angry,” he remarked.
“Your snakes looked happy,” she replied, unable to look at him in the face.
He reached up and placed a finger under her chin, forcing her to look up. Radiating sadness, he sighed. “Now’s not the time for this.”
His fingers smelled of sweat and blood, and Chaeli’s stomach churned. “You were enjoying beating that man. I saw it in your face. It was horrible.”
“It’s consensual, Chaeli. It’s sport. Of course I enjoy it.” There was a hint of confusion in his voice, and uncertainty. “Let’s talk about this another time, please.”
Not wanting an argument, she nodded and so he thanked her. She moved away, watching as he opened the note and read it, a smile unfolding on his lips. Chaeli pulled a face, curled her toes in her shoes and dug her nails into her palms.
A spark of pain deep inside almost winded her, and the pale woman appeared at Anya’s side, shaking her head silently, a finger at her lips, distracting her from Nathan. Was she demonstruck? Mad? Chaeli squeezed her eyes shut and rubbed her temples, whispering words of prayer. On reopening her eyes, the woman was gone once again.
Soon it was obvious what the gold coin entry price had bought: the room was transformed into a sumptuous banquet hall. Drapes covered the crude walls, and tall vases filled with flowers were dotted around the room, platters of meat and vegetables covering the tables that were carried into the space. Chaeli found herself seated between two traders, a potter and a tailor, at the opposite end of the hall to Anya and Nathan. Conversation flowed freely, and she spoke of Alingale and home. Every so often they would ask how she came to be in the city, but she remembered the story so well practiced with Anya: she was training to be a medic and heard that the King’s forbidden needed a female nurse. Whenever Anya was mentioned, the men would frown and pray to the gods for her health and beauty. Anya was loved, of that there was no doubt.
After food came the entertainment, and the dancers of Agassia returned, beckoning those they had marked with garlands to dance with them. Chaeli’s ears turned pink with embarrassment as she stomped and stumbled around the floor with the tiny people. Twice she stood on their bare feet, and twice she begged for forgiveness, but in true Agassian style, they laughed and kissed her hands, telling her there was nothing to forgive. When they finally released her, she collapsed on her bench with a laugh and politely declined many offers to dance as the night crawled on.
“Would you care to dance?”
Chaeli turned to see the Porton, Merlon Derrick, holding out his hand and grinning. His appearance was softer now, dressed as he was in typical ship leathers and boots. As he smiled, the corners of his eyes crinkled, lighting up his face. There was a large bruise to his cheek, far larger than Nathan’s, and his bottom lip was split and puffy.
“I can’t dance.”
“I wouldn’t say that. Denna has blessed you, I am sure.”
“Did you not see me squash the feet of the Agassians?” She sipping on her mead and grinned back, tapping her feet. “I’m as graceful as a tree bear.”
“But twice as pretty, and”—he paused, looking over at Nathan—“it would lessen the sting of my loss if I got to dance with a pretty young woman.”
Chaeli smiled into her cup. Nathan was deep in conversation with his sister and a lord, and her two dinner companions were off dancing, their attention purely on one another.
“One dance.”

Merlon was light on his toes, and as charming as a Porton sailor could be. His humour gentle, his touch gentlemanly, he told Chaeli of the many secular islands he had visited, and about his life on Lysena Rock – the black sand and sulfur that permeated everything was as welcome to him as rain in Mischla. He guided her around the dance floor, and she relaxed his arms and told him of her dream to visit Porton, to stare at the dark skies from the top of Lady Volcano. By the time the third dance ended, she suddenly noticed how close they now danced, and how his hands rested on her waist and shoulder with ease.
“Thank you for the dance, or should I say dances. I shall be the envy of every man in the room,” he remarked.
“I think not. Most men are grateful to you, for you’ve saved their toes from being ground into mincemeat by a woman my size.”
“Gods woman! You’re half the size of me,” he teased, pinching at her waist. “We like real women in Porton, not like these inlanders.” He jerked his head around the room and grimaced. “All skin and bone and worrying ’bout what they eat. No wonder they don’t smile.” One woman heard him and scowled, dragging her partner to another dance space and muttering about Porton rudeness.
Chaeli bit her lip and shook her head in mock exasperation. “Merlon, hush!”
“My grandmamma, she was a tiny thing, but ate for all of Ibea. ‘Have a little of what you want, when you want’, that was her saying. She’s as old as a dragon now, and twice as scary, mind. Anyway, she says women need not worry about stupid things like weight. You, Miss Chaeli, are a delight. We’re all made different. Nothing we can do about that. It’s the gods’ will.”
Gods’ will, Chaeli echoed silently, thinking again of Acelle, and the man she had left at the hands of the demon. Shame engulfed her; while she danced and laughed with this man, her sister lay cold and dead.
She faltered, and Merlon gripped her a little tighter. “You all right, Miss Chaeli? You’ve gone the colour of a sea eel.”
“I’m fine. I just . . . you just reminded me of something.” And just like that, she found herself telling him about the death of Acelle, carefully omitting details of the demon and instead concentrating on the horror of finding her body. Merlon listened in silence, his dark eyes connecting with hers. It was unnerving to be able to speak so openly and freely with a stranger, but the words wouldn’t stop until she confessed her guilt at enjoying the company of others and the feeling of happiness.
“It’s no sin to celebrate life, Miss Chaeli. Your sister wouldn’t want you miserable. Sometimes we need to cry and feel, because if we don’t it will eat us alive.”
With these words, a tear slid down her cheek and he brushed it away gently with a calloused thumb.
“See now, your tears honour her.”
The back of her neck prickled again, and when she looked over her shoulder, the strange woman stood by the band, flickering in and out of focus as dancers crossed Chaeli’s line of sight. She turned back, trying to ignore the woman, and smiled at her partner again.
“It’s been four dances now. I’ve done good,” he said, clearly proud.
“Thank you, Merlon, for listening to me.” She smiled up at him, and spoke gently with warmth.
“Pleasure. But it looks like I’m about to be replaced.”
“Sorry?”
The sailor wasn’t given the option to reply, for Nathan appeared, slapping Merlon on the back and thanking him for the fight. Merlon stopped dancing and stood in the middle of the dance floor; Chaeli found herself smiling uneasily as the men discussed the technical aspects of the first round. She tried to slip away, but Nathan grabbed her wrist and stopped midsentence:
“Chaeli, wait.”
Chaeli thanked Merlon once more, who bowed to them both and kissed her hand before departing. Nathan pulled her closer.
“Shall we dance?”
“I was dancing,” she replied, still angry with him though she struggled to recall why. Ah yes, the fight. And the note.
“I’m sure there are other women who would be flattered to dance with the champion of the evening,” she said, and though she protested, he held her close and she found her hands on his waist.
“I’m sure there are.” He glided them around the room now. “But I would be flattered to dance with you.”
“And why’s that then?”
“Because you don’t want to dance with me.”
He made no sense, and irritated that she didn’t understand him, she clamped her lips shut and they danced in silence. Her back was rigid and she stumbled often, crushing his toes and causing them to stop and start over and over.
“Relax, Chaeli. I won’t bite.”
“No, but they might.”
Though they were covered in a white linen shirt, the tail of a serpent disappeared under his neckline. She pulled back, widening the space between them. The smell of blood and sweat still lingered when he moved, and she found herself half-wishing that she still danced with Merlon.
“I’m sorry. I didn’t want you to come,” he said, noting her unhappiness. He stopped and let her go. “I’m in control of them. I have to be.”
He turned and strode away, not looking back as the bear- and lizard-adorned lady made a beeline for him, hooking her arm through his and leading him out of the room.

The following morning, with her tongue stuck to the roof of her mouth and a pounding headache, Chaeli woke late to find both Anya and Nathan downstairs reading. As she took a seat on the bench next to them, a smile tickled her, followed by a giggle, for Anya had covered every bruise on Nathan’s face with a purple paste that smelled of vomit and pears.
“It’ll speed up healing,” Anya explained, sipping at her fruit tea with a straight face.
Nathan muttered under his breath and continued to read, hunched over the bench with an air of both indignity and resignation. Chaeli poured herself a cup of cool tea and grimaced as it mixed with stale mead and washed down her throat.
“Someone had a little too much to drink last night,” Anya remarked, not looking up.
With a grunt, Chaeli pulled over the plate of sugar bread and nibbled. “Didn’t think it would be that strong,” she said in between mouthfuls.
“Well, Merlon seemed to love the attention, and if you ever proclaim not to be able to dance again, I shall cut out your silver tongue! You were the talk of the night. Where did you learn to jig like that?”
The hazy memories returned in broken pieces: dancing on the table, dancing on Merlon’s shoulders, singing along to the Porton sea-tunes and then . . . oh no . . . she groaned and shook her head as she recalled climbing the Agassians’ rope. Anya deliberately refused to make eye contact, but Nathan stared at her, though it was clear he fought a smile.
“I can’t believe I did that. I can’t believe you let me do that.”
“You weren’t the only one, Chaeli. At our end of the table we had the chief Magistrate seeing how many grapes he could fit in his mouth before choking. I mean, seriously…”
They continued to reminisce, and Chaeli relaxed, surrounding herself with the positive cheer and happiness that the Games brought. Though there was a small part of her that niggled with shame for having fun, the rest of her was grateful. Every laugh helped soothe the pain and quash the memory of her home.
“Chaeli, I’m training this afternoon and Merlon’s asked if you want to have dinner with him this evening,” said Nathan.
“Oh! Err . . .”
“Don’t worry, I’ll be there too. It’s like a consolation dinner, the families of the winner and loser often have dinner together to ensure there’s no bad blood. Nathan will be there too. Nat, why didn’t you mention that?”
Nathan didn’t reply and instead slowly turned a page in his book.
“Dinner sounds great, though I don’t have any money to pay—”
“Don’t be silly, Chaeli. Nathan is paying for us all.”
Raising an eyebrow, Nathan nodded. “And I’m buying you a dress as well. One that fits. No buts.”
*
As Adley and Sheiva made steady progress across the Trithian countryside, Sheiva recounted his time on Salinthos. The King grew weary, his wife listless and unresponsive, for the land and energy seemed dull and tainted, its people restless. The Queen’s bonded spirit had begged Sheiva to return to Ibea and search for the Heart of Salinthos. To Sheiva it seemed a fruitless task. The Heart had been missing for years and no creature, mortal or otherwise, knew where it had gone. Indeed, some even believed it to be destroyed. Sheiva, however, swore an oath to the Queen that he would search, and as he had spoken the words, her eyes had flashed with hope. He knew that until he found the Heart, he could not return home; to wound the Queen further would surely kill her. Adley stopped and stroked the silver dog in sympathy.
“I’m sorry, my friend. To leave such a place of paradise must be hard.”
“It is, and it isn’t. I found myself restless on Salinthos, Adley. Ibea offers such freedom and choice that I cannot resist its call.” Stopping, the dog sat and with a tilt of his head indicated the man should follow suit.
“It’s seems like yesterday I was here with Rhona,” said Adley softly. “We would often walk not far from here, away from the city, from order and rules. Here we could be ourselves.”
“Until her father found you,” Sheiva added with a lopsided, drooling grin.
“Until her father found us, indeed.” A gentle smile crossed Adley’s face for a moment, before he frowned. “And then it all changed.”
There was a silence, broken only by the birds overheard. “War changes everything. We can’t control that, my friend. Remember the happiness and forget the rest. They are at peace. They are here with you now in the touch of the wind, the dirt beneath our feet, and the sun that shines.”
Adley said nothing, and instead brushed his hands across his face and stood. “Come on, Shiv, let’s find Chaeli. I must look forward, not back.”
*
That afternoon, Nathan and Anya accompanied Chaeli through the streets of Trithia to the tailors’ sector. There, Nathan pushed her into the closest shop and began loading her arms with dresses, much to Anya’s dismay.
“Nathan . . . stop . . . wait!” Each dress he piled on Chaeli, Anya placed back on the rack, apologising to the store owner who watched them with hostility. “Nat, please. Let Chaeli pick something herself.”
The former soldier made a face, then nodded and glanced at Chaeli. “I apologise . . . I just . . . I’ve never done this before.” He sounded lost then, and Chaeli found herself suddenly interested in a glass bracelet by the window, unable to look at him.
“Nathan, when you buy a lady a dress, it’s good manners to let them try it on and decide they like it first, not just throw it at them.” Anya’s gentle reprieve did nothing to quell the heat burning in Chaeli’s cheeks, but she took a handful of dresses behind the screen in the corner to try them on.

Several hours and many shops passed, yet nothing seemed suitable for Chaeli’s frame. Her height, the envy of most women who often told her they would love her long legs, was a curse to her. Twice they had been told that her frame was ‘inelegant’ and when it was suggested that she perhaps wear trousers and a shirt, Nathan silenced that particular tailor with a look.
“We’ll try in here. Maybe Elek’s inside,” said Anya cheerfully at the door of the next shop, though even her eternal optimism seemed to be faltering.
Chaeli nodded glumly and pushed open the door. The musty smell of the shop made her cough, bringing a small man out from the back rooms.
“Ladies! Sir! Welcome. How many I help you?” Tapping his fingers together, the tailor smiled and rocked on his feet, clearly eyeing up their worth by the cut and quality of their clothes. Dismissing Chaeli, he turned to Anya and immediately noticed the cuts to her face; his smile faltered but, ever the professional, it remained in place. “Miss DeVaine.” He bowed, then stepped back on noticing who Nathan must be.
“Sir, we’re here for a dress. Something for the lady here. No trouble.” Nathan pushed Chaeli forward and the tailor nodded fervently, dropping to his knees to measure her legs and waist. So used to the lack of personal space by the previous tailors, Chaeli stood there as her limbs were contorted and pulled. Anya spent her time with the dress jewellery and Nathan stood by the door, unmoving.
“Yes, yes, I think . . . perhaps . . . I might have something to suit. You’re tall, like a Sheeman, yes? Sheeman blood, yes? Well, I have just the dress. Indeed.” The man disappeared into his stock room and reappeared with a dress before Chaeli could reply. When he held it up, Chaeli noticed his hands were shaking.
The dress was long, and an off-yellow colour. Chaeli counted at least three layers of skirting. The bodice was loose and the sleeves billowy. She touched the material and it slipped through her fingers.
“Oh Chaeli, you must try it on.” Anya appeared behind her and with no hesitation took the dress from the tailor and grabbed Chaeli’s hand, pulling her behind the screen. Chaeli beamed as the material fell modestly around her. With her hair loose and the dress clinging and falling in all the right places, she stepped out from behind the screen.
Nathan exhaled, and nodded. “Finally,” he said, his relief palpable. “We’ll take it.” Throwing a pouch at Anya, who caught it with ease, he stepped outside the shop and waited with his back turned. Chaeli changed back into her old clothes, and as the tailor wrapped up the dress, chattering away with excitement all the while, a small amount of her own excitement leaked away.
*
The braying donkey that had suddenly appeared on the ground outside his apartment window woke Dal. It was mid-afternoon and the stench of fish seemed stronger today. Gagging, he rolled and stuffed his blanket into his mouth.
Gods, that was a long night!
By the time Fyn had half-carried, half-walked him home from Mina’s, Dal had missed the fight at the Games, and so, not wanting to miss the opportunity for more drinking, he had finished a twenty-year-old bottle of some spirit or another with his brother in the apartments. He wasn’t going to pay a gold coin for a meal, not when he could drink instead. If he was honest, he had no idea what it had been, but when he left Algary, he left with half his father’s prized alcohol collection and this bottle was one of many.
Facing the wall and trying to ignore the donkey, Dal groaned as sickness rocked through him, and when finally he couldn’t ignore the waves of nausea any longer, he sat up and downed the jug of warm water by his bed.
The room swam and the walls bent and curved. With a moment of clarity, he picked up the discarded bottle and realised what it was: Fae Fiend, named so for the fact that it tricked the drinker into thinking they would be fine the next day. Evidently not so, Fae Fiend worked slowly, and after twelve hours, the hangover would truly begin. Putting his hands to his mouth, Dal crawled to the chamber-pot and, with a muttered curse, promptly passed out.
*
“Miss Chaeli, you look wonderful,” said Merlon Derrick, bowing down and kissing her hand. Chaeli blushed and pulled the big Porton into a hug. The Leaping Leopard Inn was loud and packed, and as Merlon grasped Nathan’s hand and shook it warmly, he shouted, “Come, I have a table in the garden, we’ll not be disturbed.”
Nathan, followed both Anya and Chaeli, looked from side to side to watch the many faces in the crowds. Several patrons recognised him and raised a glass, but there was no animosity, for they too were hunted. The Leopard’s reputation led it to be a place for outlaws and criminals, those that flouted the laws and had prices on their heads. Civilians were to surrender weapons at the gates with the guards, but here Nathan counted many swords, daggers, bows, and crossbows. The King’s Guard weren’t foolish enough to come here, and so it was a place where they could dine at ease.
The last to sit, Nathan found himself opposite Chaeli, next to Anya. Merlon took the seat to Chaeli’s right and was already pouring the wine.
“My family are in Porton, so it’s just me tonight. But don’t worry, DeVaine, I’ll make sure that purse feels the pain my face does.” The sailor was cheerful, and his eyes twinkled with mirth. “You know I’m kidding. Next round is mine.”
“Next round?” Chaeli asked. She frowned and turned to Merlon, the white lily behind her left ear slipping. Nathan reached up and pushed it back, causing her to jump and grab her ear, touching his fingers. He apologised and drew away.
“There’s three rounds to each game,” Merlon said, as if he had not noticed the awkward exchange. “Your man here won the first round, but we fight again in a month. Not sure where yet, mind. Have you had the location?”
Nathan shook his head. “No, I’ve been told it will arrive ten days beforehand.”
“Bit of a shit, that. Why all the secrecy I don’t know.”
“I hope it’s here again, it was such good fun last night. I haven’t laughed so much in years,” Anya said, passing around the menus.
They continued to talk, Merlon telling them of his time at sea, Chaeli hanging on his every word, and Anya speaking of her love for practising medicine. Nathan didn’t feel the need to speak. What could he tell them? He had moved from realm to realm, village to town and back again. A few months in each place before moving on again, such was the life of an outcast. Only King Cobey had shown him pity, but not friendship, and he had told Nathan that if asked, he would deny all knowledge of his presence in the realm. When Merlon spoke of his family, Nathan found himself watching Chaeli, and the way she stared at Merlon. Her face didn’t move, her lips in a thin line, but her eyes filled with tears. The only way he could stop himself from reaching out and wiping them away was to look at his sister beseechingly; Anya changed the conversation back to Trithia and the Games. As they ate, the colour returned to Chaeli’s cheeks and the smile to her eyes and lips.

“Chaeli, before we retire, would you accompany me outside? I have an errand and would like some company,” Nathan asked as he laid out the blankets later that night. She was tired and just wanted to sleep, but she felt indebted to him for the dress, his hospitality, and so much more. She wanted to show him that she didn’t feel prejudiced against him for his past.
“Of course,” she replied.
They left together, Anya bolting the door behind them. Chaeli shivered as the cool air tickled her skin. Nathan moved closer. She could feel the warmth of his breath on her neck.
“I have something to show you. Follow me,” he murmured. Chaeli could smell him, a disturbing mixture of exotic spices, and something she couldn’t put her finger on. Another spice, perhaps, but not something she recognised. He moved quickly and silently through the alleyways, and the voice in Chaeli’s head reminded her that he was a trained killer. She ignored it and concentrated on keeping up with him. After twenty minutes her sides were aching and she was out of breath.
Ye gods, where are we going?
Nathan paused and turned around; he held a finger to his lips. He pulled her close, his chest pressed against her back. The smell of soap mingled with the scent of sweat. He pointed between the gaps in the alleyway towards a small glowing light. Chaeli strained to see what he was looking at. A patrol of soldiers, perhaps thirty or so, stood in a compact and disciplined group.
“I’ve seen two other patrols today. Initially I thought it was a training exercise, but word on the street is they are searching for someone,” he breathed.
“Who?” she whispered back.
“That’s what we’re here to find out.” He moved his hands around her waist and pulled her further into the shadows; they could hear the soldiers gossiping.
“Cap’n says we’re to start lookin’ for ’em in the mornin’. Issat DeVaine traitor and some bitch wif ’im. Ten gold pieces for the man who finds ’em!” The soldier let out a harsh laugh. “The girl is to be kept alive. The King don’t care what ’appens to the traitor.”
“What’s so important about the girl?” asked another, lighting a pipe and drawing in deeply.
“Dunno, do I?” said the first, snorting and spitting on the road. “Maybe she’s a tasty piece.” They laughed, the younger choking on his smoke. “‘I also ’eard the King’s priest wants to see ’er too.”
Nathan grabbed Chaeli’s upper arm. Startled, she almost cried out, but as fast as lightning Nathan’s other hand covered her mouth.
“Let’s go,” he whispered. Slipping into the shadows, they made their way back to Anya’s. They knocked softly and waited for her to pull the bolts back and open the door. Nathan practically threw Chaeli through the opening.
“We’ve got to go, and we’ve got to go quickly,” he declared, pacing up and down the room.
“What’s happened?” asked Anya.
“The guard are looking for me. Someone has tipped them off.” He cursed, punching the bench in anger. “And Chaeli, they’re looking for her too.”
Anya looked at her. “Why?”
“Someone must have told them she was with me – that snake-sworn guard on the gate.” Nathan was furious now. “He sold us out.”
“Where will you go?”
“I don’t know, Velen maybe. I could get some work in the pits.”
“Velen?” cried Anya. “That’s a death sentence!”
“Where else can I go?”
“Come with me to the library at Lindor,” Chaeli blurted.
Nathan looked at her darkly. “Why the library?”
“I need to research the scriptures. The demon said something about me not knowing them well enough. I need to know about the two brothers and why the Kingdoms were split.”
Nathan fell silent. It was clear that the prospect of sitting down to read through obscure and dusty books didn’t appeal to him, but it would be more comfortable than the mines or pits of Velen. He weighed the two up for a few moments.
“Fine, we’ll go to the library, but we can’t go together. I’ll get you out of the city tonight and meet up with you on the road.”
“What about your men?”
“I’ll go to them. We’ll disband for now – until this has all calmed down.”
He grabbed his cloak and swept it around her shoulders. “Take this, you need to keep warm.” He turned to Anya. “Do you have some coin?” She nodded and dashed up the stairs. Nathan turned back to Chaeli. “I will join you, but I can’t leave tonight. I need to find my men, to ensure their safety, do you understand?”
She nodded. She was afraid of being on her own again, but couldn’t tell him that now. More than anything she did not want to appear helpless before him.
“I have twenty silver and two gold,” called Anya, “will that do?”
“Plenty,” he called back avoiding Chaeli’s gaze. Anya ran into the room and moved straight to her balms. She scanned the shelves grabbing many pots and small jars, which she stuffed into a pack and thrust it into Chaeli’s arms.
“Here, take these. The aloe balm is for your wound. Apply it twice each day until you are healed. It will prevent scarring. The jar of elderflower tea is for nerves, and there are several small packs of seeds and berries for the journey.”
“Anya, I can’t take your supplies, what about your patients?”
“I have plenty.” Anya stood on her tiptoes and kissed Chaeli’s cheek.
Nathan had his hand on the door. “Time to go.”
She followed him into the night, looking back and raising a hand to Anya. “Thank you,” she mouthed as the small woman closed the door behind them.
Nathan took her hand in his. Chaeli thought she recognised the route from a few days earlier, but the courtyards all looked the same in the dark. Nathan was sure though. He led her through the city at a rapid rate, and without a pause or hesitation. The city was beautiful at night. Chaeli had a pang of regret; there was so much to see here and now she had to leave. The moonlight bounced off the glass dome of the castle and made her wish she could visit there. Her gaze was caught by a tall, copper building; even in the dark it was shining.
“What’s that?” she asked, a strange feeling arising in her at the sight. Nathan glanced at the building, neither stopping or slowing his pace.
“Do you really want to know?” he said as he strode over the pebbled arts arena, where several small troupes were practising their acts by the light of blazing torches. Chaeli dodged fearfully as juggling balls flew past.
“Yes.”
“It’s an offering temple to Metlina.”
Chaeli gasped. “Sacrifices?”
“Yes.”

She is so naïve, he thought. He was both drawn to her and disturbed by her, this unusual looking girl with no standing, no background, no papers, and demons chasing her. He couldn’t understand why the King was interested in her – surely it was simply her association with Nathan himself which put her in danger? Stirm was a vindictive man and would stop at nothing to punish Nathan, maybe especially through those he held dear.
But did he hold Chaeli dear? Anya liked her, and that would have been enough for him. If there was more than that it was surely doomed, for Chaeli had been afraid of him ever since she had seen the inking. He thought – hoped at least - she would understand in time, would see he was no longer that man. A vindictive irritation wormed its way to the surface of his mind. He tightened his grip on her hand.
“What’s wrong?” she asked.
“Nothing,” he snapped back.
The smell of the Sink hit them, the wind carrying the stench of waste, of faeces, of urine and rotting food, the unmistakable stink of human and animal degradation. Nathan quickened his pace. As they reached the outskirts of the solid buildings the roads ended and Nathan negotiated them over the broken stones and mud. At the stone wall Nathan stopped and looked at Chaeli.
“I’m going to lift you onto my shoulders and you’ll have to lever yourself up and over,” he explained. “Can you do that?”
She looked at the wall; it was a good fifteen to sixteen feet tall. “I might be able to.”
Nathan looked her up and down. “You’ll be fine,” he said.
She smiled awkwardly. “Thanks. How are you getting over?”
