Nathan hesitated. “Chaeli, I must see my men. You’ll have to navigate the Sink yourself.”
“What? I can’t do that! You told me people get raped and killed there, and that’s in the daytime.”
“I know what I said, but there is no choice.”
“There’s always a choice! I’ll stay here in the city. I’ll be fine.” She crossed her arms over her chest and stared at him. “I can’t face the Sink on my own, Nathan, I just can’t.”
Nathan rubbed his face, and blew out a long breath. Deep down he knew she was right, but he just wanted to get her away from Stirm – the Sink was nothing compared to the vengeance of the King.
“What if one of my men came with you?” he said. “I could send Adyam along. He’s quick with a sword and has eyes like a hawk. You would be safe with him.”
Chaeli sighed with relief and nodded.
Nathan cursed. “Ah, no. He’s on the other side of the city, there is no way I can get to him.”
Her panic rose again. “Then what?”
“You have to go Chaeli, please. As soon as you hit the other side, run. Just a straight line and run – three kilometres at the most. You can do it.”
She shook her head. “No, Nathan, no I can’t. I can’t!”
“Chaeli, don’t you realise, I can’t keep you safe? I can’t keep anyone safe.” He shook her, but she pushed him hard in the chest. He slipped slightly and dug his fingers into her arms.
“Stop shaking me, Nathan. Get your hands off me! Just lift me over the damned wall, then you’ll be free of all obligations!”
Nathan released her arms, his face a mask. “Step on my shoulders.”
She nodded and secured her heavy, cumbersome pack on her back. Nathan lifted her up, grunting as she kicked him in the head, which caused her to feel first smug, and then shameful. She scrambled and clawed at the stone, and at last managed to slither up onto the top of the wall. With one last desperate push of strength, she hauled her stomach onto the summit.
“Chaeli,” called Nathan softly, “I will find you. I won’t leave you, I promise.”
She gave no reply, but turned around. The drop was a lot greater that side.
“Remember, just run!”
With that she dropped into the Sink.
Nathan sprinted away through the shadows. He wanted to stay in the city, to protect Anya. Now the soldiers knew he was here, she wouldn’t be safe, but Anya had a better chance if he was gone. Why did he promise Chaeli he would come? Why, in the name of the gods, had he done that? He couldn’t understand any of his actions of the last few days; he was usually so calm, so in control. She confused him . . . and was constantly in his thoughts.
*
Her feet sank as she hit the mud on the other side of the wall and she crouched down. Pitch black at first, it was impossible to differentiate the shadows and the buildings. As the hairs on the back of her neck stood up and the wind carried whispers, Chaeli looked around for the voices, waiting for them to pounce, but nothing came. Exhaling in relief, she dragged her feet from the sludge and jogged towards the flat plains and the road. Here, she started running, her feet slapping the ground as she moved. Soon she felt as though her lungs would explode. She was exhausted. She couldn’t even shout apologies to the people she knocked past. The constant battle against the resistance of the mud worked muscles she didn’t know she had. She paused, leaning over to catch her breath, and wiped the sweat from her eyes. The buildings around her were tightly packed; she had wasted precious time weaving in and out of huts and tripping over debris on the path.
She scanned the route as far as she could see: dark outlines of people marred her view but as her eyes adjusted and the black gave way to shades of grey, she tightened the pack on her back and started off again, uncertainly this time. She was lost; she couldn’t see the wastelands, and the smells of the Sink were beginning to make her feel sick. She slowed and held a hand to her face in a vain attempt to staunch the odours, but it did nothing to block out the sounds of weeping and begging that carried on the wind.
She prayed for guidance, for Elek, but there was nothing. Suddenly she screamed as she was shoved to the ground, face first in the muck. Her attacker lay on top of her, heavy and crushing, and Chaeli sank further, stones digging into her skin. Fear gripped her and silenced the cry inside.
She thrashed and heard a deep grunt as her heels connected with her assailant. The weight shifted and, grabbing at the mud, she dragged herself forward and rolled onto her back. The straps from her pack pinched her skin as she pulled herself to her feet. Each breath and movement seemed to take an eternity. Then she saw him.
His face was covered with scars and fresh wounds, his hair matted and knotted. As he rolled to his feet it was clear she was taller than him, but that did nothing to ease the palpitations that rocked through her. She wanted to run, but couldn’t turn her back on him, so she stood rooted to the spot. He laughed and launched himself at her again, his hands grasping at her skin. The smell of him, the closeness of him, violated her senses. He pulled at her dress and she could do nothing to stop him. She was weak, useless. He pushed her to the ground again and she winced as he collapsed on top of her.
It was then her body chose to respond. Her muscles relaxed and she felt blood rush to her limbs. She clenched her fists and in one swift movement she punched him in the head. His fumbling ceased as she hit him over and over again using both fists now. He scrambled off her and she leaped to her feet, kicking out at him.
No longer laughing, he groaned and sobbed as her foot connected with his stomach. Her fear gave way to anger and the rage roared and coursed through her veins. As she reared back to kick him again she paused and faltered. What was she doing?
“Forgiveness is an attribute of the strong. To seek revenge shifts the deserved right into a great wrong.”
The words of her scriptures echoed in her mind and she drew back. The man tried to pull himself to his knees before falling back and crying. Giving up, he started to crawl towards the sludge and mud, apologising over and over again. Trembling, she turned and ran away, passing the last scattering of homes before she hit the wasteland. There she stopped, and bent over, her hands on her knees, gulping in deep breaths. The stench of the land seeped into her and she could still hear the thrum of voices from the Sink, the howling of the dogs. Ye gods!
The touch of her attacker still crawled along her skin. She could still smell him, feel him. She shuddered.
“Hey you!”
Chaeli turned and clenched her fists in anticipation of another attack, a soldier walked towards her in his bright red uniform, lantern in his left hand, his right hand ready on the hilt of his sword. Oh Gods! No! They’ve found me.
She couldn’t run any more, so she mustered a smile and tried to relax. With the memory of her attack fresh and stirring up conflicting emotions, she tried to appeal to him the way she’d seen the miller’s daughter act around the older boys of the village. “Yes sir?” She batted her eyelashes and thrust out her chest. All the while hoping and praying to Elek for luck, for the guard not to ask to see her papers.
“What’s the hurry? Why were you running?” he asked, holding the lantern up to stare at her face.
Chaeli hesitated, her mind blank. “I—”
“There you are!” called a large man striding towards them. “We wondered if you’d make it.” He slapped her so hard on the back that she stumbled.
The soldier glanced at the man and then back to Chaeli. “You know her?”
“Yes, my good man, she’s the seamstress for the troupe. I sent her to get some cloth from the city before we left.” He looked at Chaeli and winked. “Did you get it m’dear?”
“Er, no, sorry.”
“What’s the name of your troupe?” asked the soldier.
“Danven’s Dancing Delights. We specialise in bizarre and extraordinary requests.”
“Danven’s eh? I’ve heard of you.” The soldier leered. “Some pretty women you’ve got.” He grabbed at his crotch absently, and spat on the ground, glancing at Chaeli with renewed interest. She swallowed.
Forgive me, my gods, she prayed. Please forgive me, Mother, forgive me, Father. You know why I must.
“Heard they’re real good too,” the soldier added tearing his gaze from Chaeli’s chest and back to the man.
“The best.” He winked at the soldier. “Alas, sir, we are preparing to leave. Heading to the smaller towns.”
“Shame that,” muttered the soldier. “All right, off you go then. And don’t you go running anymore, miss, it gets the wrong sort of attention.” With a last glance at Chaeli, he nodded to the man and walked off.
Chaeli stared at her unexpected saviour. “Thanks,” she said. He was a huge portly figure dressed in bright silks. His hair was jet black, but his neat, white trimmed beard gave away a sign of his true age.
“I love to help a damsel in distress,” he replied, looking her up and down.
“Well, thank you again, I must be going.” She was uncomfortable now, and disliked the way he looked at her. She turned and walked slowly away.
“Ah, a lady such as yourself shouldn’t be alone at night. Perhaps you’d like some company?”
“No, I’m fine thank you,” she called over her shoulder.
“My caravans are just over the hill this way. We have plenty of food, drink and companionship. The ladies would love to meet you, and take care of you,” he said, falling into step beside her.
“No. Thank you.”
“Such a shame. Well, perhaps we will meet again someday. I will speak of your beauty to the scholars at Lindor.”
“Lindor? You’re going to Lindor?” she asked, pulling up short.
“Yes, one of the Lords there requested our attendance.” He smiled, took off his hat and held it to his chest. “Would you care to join us?”
Chaeli chewed her bottom lip. Lindor was at least three weeks by foot, whereas travelling with the caravans she could be there in a week; and it would be much safer. She weighed up the odds of Nathan actually returning for her, or even finding her. She couldn’t rely on him forever. With some trepidation she made up her mind.
He held out his arm. “I am Danven. Come, meet my girls.”
“Chaeli,” she replied. She took his arm and smiled. What in all the hells was she getting herself into now?
*
Eli smiled to himself. The rats had provided him with some useful information: Chaeli had run from the city and was now with Danven. Wonderful. He had sent Malo to the King, and according to the beast, Stirm was tripping over himself to help. That maggot was always trying to gain favour with the Underworld.
The shrieks and moans of the condemned pushed through the many doors and corridors that led down to his dungeons. Kings, peasants, farmers, lords – mortal hierarchies had no meaning in his Underworld, all were equally damned. Their cries formed a sweet chorus, a proclamation of love to him. Each blow they received from the whips and chains of his demons was a gift to him. Each death they relived, a hundred deaths of agony and pleasure, a sacrifice and declaration of loyalty to him.
The years of the truce had worn him down, but now he felt refreshed and alive. Sipping from a crystal goblet, he reflected on the letter he had sent to Daro. His brother hadn’t acknowledged it, of course, but still Eli revelled in disclosing that Chaeli was his, that she belonged to him. Not only had Eli owned Amelia, he now had Amelia’s daughter, her flesh and blood.
Amelia . . .
From the moment Daro had introduced her to Eli, he had wanted her. His jealousy towards his brother intensified, washing through him, burning and growing into an unquenchable fury. Not only was Daro the oldest and most revered, he had the purest and deepest love of Amelia. Now, the memory of her body, the touch of her skin, distracted Eli; he needed a release.
“Metlina, come!”
Within seconds he could sense her energy pulsing through the room. She appeared and knelt in front of her lord with her head bowed and arms outstretched.
“Rise.”
She stood, keeping her head low, and he stared at her tight copper curls. Today she wore a blood-red gown with a black bone diadem; he was sure the jewellery had been a gift from him many years ago, though the memory was hazy.
“Kiss your liege,” he demanded. She raised her head and leaned towards his face. He embraced her with a vicious passion. Their mouths met, lips and teeth clashing together. He loved mortal senses, the fragile life which brought the constant awareness of danger. He pulled her closer, running his tongue along her bottom lip. She whimpered, and he bit down hard. The Goddess of Pain yelped and he tightened his grip on her hair. Metlina touched her mouth, her fingers coming away with drops of red; a gentle moan of pleasure escaped her lips. Eli brushed her cheekbone with his thumb, then licked the blood away.
“Kiss me again, my beautiful Metlina,” he whispered. He was hungry for her and as she reached up to him, he shook his head.
“Not there.” He began to unlace his trousers. She dropped to her knees in anticipation.
*
“Adders, please, I need to stop,” whined the dog. They had been walking for almost two days and he was exhausted. His paws were covered in debris from the road, and the pads were bruised; worse – his stomach growled. They had at last reached the rolling hills and green countryside of Trithia after crossing the wastelands; the surrounding woods were plentiful in small wildlife, but Sheiva’s ability to transform only stretched so far. He could sniff out the food, however the ability to stalk their meal while it moved still proved a challenge and Adley’s hunting skills were rusty, for he hadn’t the need to eat or drink for centuries. So far, they had survived on an elderly rabbit that had needed hours of cooking to be edible and a handful of apples stolen from a farmer’s field.
“Look, we’re nearly at the city,” said Adley, pointing, the relief almost overwhelming him. He, too, needed to rest, and to eat. He was still stiff and sore from being healed, and he felt old.
The dog wagged his tail. “Well that’s good – the sooner we find her, the sooner I can change out of this mangy mutt suit.”
“But you’re so cute as a dog, Shiv.”
“Careful, I might just bite you.”
Adley chuckled. “It’s almost sunrise, people will start to trade soon. When we hit the city I can’t talk to you other than to give commands.”
“Charming,” snorted the dog. “But I understand.” He took a couple of sniffs at the ground. “She’s been here.”
“Good. Hopefully we’ll find her in the city.” Adley jumped from the small ledge on which he stood to the scrub below. Sheiva followed, sniffing at the wind and snapping at wasps that hovered too close to his muzzle.
As they hit the Sink, Sheiva growled. Adley soothed him with a pat to the head.
“I know, Shiv. It gets better, I promise.” The cool breeze from the sunrise soon disappeared. Adley saw the sick and the old, saw children scurrying around filthy huts searching for any scraps they could find. Two young lads beat each other for a half-loaf of mouldy bread. There had been a time when a philanthropic king ruled, when no one went hungry, and all were equal in his eyes. Children had been educated by the scribes and those unfortunate enough to be homeless were offered lodging in return for work at the castle. The equilibrium had been maintained, the people happy. Where had it all gone wrong? In truth, he knew: when House Windthorn had taken the throne by force at the time of the signing of the divine treaty four hundred years ago, Trithia had fallen into disarray. The gods were too busy, too distracted to grant their grace, and so the land and the people had suffered, were suffering still under the rule of the last of the Windthorn line.
Sheiva cocked his head and indicated the west gate. Adley nodded and the pair made their way over the mud, passing a tatty whorehouse with a makeshift nursery, little more than a lean-to, tacked on the side. The calls from the women and the groans from the customers could be heard clearly through the paper-thin walls.
“Papers,” said the bored soldier at the gate. He sat staring into the distance and holding out his hand.
Adley removed his pack and dug out the papers. “Looks like it’s going to be a nice day,” he said.
“What?” The soldier stuck a finger into his ear and scratched. He pulled it out, sniffed it, then wiped the wax on his stained tunic.
“The weather, it looks good. Nice day and so forth.”
“I suppose. I’m stuck here though, aren’t I?” The soldier gave the papers a cursory glance and handed them back.
“Well, good day,” Adley said, and they walked into the city. The solider paid no attention and went back to his staring. Sheiva sniffed and began casting about, moving in widening semi-circles. Eventually he found a scent that led them both deep into the city.
*
When Nathan returned to Anya, he fell upon her, hugging her in relief.
“Is she safe?”
Nathan nodded. “I said I would meet her and accompany her to Lindor.” Anya said nothing but squeezed him tighter. “I’ll miss you, little sister.”
“Miss you too.” She let go and freed herself from his embrace. “Do you think Stirm will know you were here?” she asked, her voice a whisper as she lowered herself into a chair, her golden hair hanging over her shoulders disguising her face.
“He knows I would come to you.”
Anya nodded. “Do you think he will be angry?”
A surge of shame and anger choked Nathan then. “I don’t know, Anya,” was all he could say. Deep down he knew Stirm would be furious. “Come with me. Come away to Lindor.”
She shook her head. “No, I can’t leave my patients, they need me.”
“You’ll be safe. We can be together.”
“No, I won’t let Stirm win. He always wins,” she whispered, wringing her hands together as though trying to squeeze away the fear. She looked up at him again. The silver and red lines glared in the early light of day streaming through the opaque glass door. Nathan sat next to her on the bench and held her hand.
“I don’t know when I’ll see you again,” he said with a heavy heart.
She patted his hand. “You’d best leave, sunrise is coming and the soldiers will be out in force.”
“Stay safe. Any problems, and you leave straight away.” He knew she would never leave those in need; she had never let him look after her. He grabbed a small pack and some supplies, giving Anya a quick kiss on the cheek. As he opened the door he checked both left and right, then disappeared into a passing crowd of workers on their way to the castle to serve their king.
*
Adley followed Sheiva through the city, exhausted and longing for a hot meal and a warm bed. He thought of Chaeli and how he would explain his presence, and what was going on. He realised that every explanation he thought of sounded ludicrous. Something inside warned him his feelings for her were wrong; it slithered around his thoughts, but Adley pushed it away, pushed it deep down. He had been so disciplined with his emotions for centuries, but these yearnings renewed in him. Penella knew; he had expected a warning from the goddess, but none came. It provoked a strange and unwarranted hope. He would allow himself to feel again.
The streets gradually filled with people and the noises from every direction distracted Adley from his thoughts. The atmosphere of the city had changed since he last visited, but the roads and basic layout remained the same. He stumbled past the commemorative iron Dragon Wars’ statue; the twenty foot creation showing the final battle between the dragon clans. He realised just how long he had spent away from the world of mortals. The image of the soldier, sword raised and arcing towards the neck of the dragon, was hauntingly familiar.
Things had seemed so very different, yet so very similar when he had first stepped foot in the city. He marvelled at the progression in medicines and the arts, but so much in the way of life seemed to have slowed down, barely changing in all this time. During many long discussions with Prince Daro, he had questioned the stilted growth on Ibea and the lack of development or evolution. Adley believed there was reluctance to change, but Daro refused to accept that the mortals were idle or uninspired. Adley recalled a throwaway comment Daro had made during one of their many heated discussions: ‘Mortals always push the boundaries of their beliefs and accepted way of life, but they need to release themselves from those shackles. They are afraid, Adley, afraid that their dreams and designs will be viewed as heresy or wrongdoing. But all those thoughts and dreams form their growth. I see wonderful promise in mortals.’
It was only now he truly understood what Daro had meant: his divine prince wanted the mortals to grow and enrich their lives with progress, even at the cost of his own existence. Daro placed mortal humanity higher than all other creatures, including the immortals.
Adley remained deep in thought as the dog searched around a cobbled square. There was a small tug at his left pocket and he reached down quickly and grabbed a small chubby wrist. A high pitched squeal cut through the air around him and he looked down at the boy whose hand was trapped in his pocket. His eyes were wide, his fat face smeared with sugar.
“What are you doing, boy?” Adley growled with as much menace as he could muster.
“I–I . . . sorry sir.” The boy hung his head in shame.
“Do you not know stealing is a sin?” He maintained his grip and the boy twisted his body and scuffed his feet against the cobbles, refusing to look up.
“Yes sir, I’m just hungry ’tis all. Me mam’s still asleep and she won’t cook me no morning meal. I had to eat the last bit o’ sugar bread, and she’s goin’ to go mad at me when she wakes up.” He looked up at Adley with tears in his eyes, but the man wasn’t fooled. He gripped the wrist tighter and reached down to the inner tunic pocket of the fat lad and produced a handmade leather pouch. Coins clinked together and Adley weighed the amount.
“Seems to me you have enough here to eat twenty breakfasts and replace the sugar bread for your mother.” He held the pouch in his hand and stared long and hard at the lad. The boy shrugged and the tears dried up as he maintained the level stare of the older man. He stuck out his tongue.
“Seems like you need to learn some humility, boy. Perhaps I should hand you over to that guard there.” He gave a quick nod to a soldier across from them. “He can teach you, of that I’m sure.”
“Please, no. I won’t do it again. I’m sorry, sir, please, no.” The boy wiggled and Adley turned his back to the guard to hide them, not wanting to draw attention to himself.
“Now I don’t believe that. I suggest you go straight home and pray for forgiveness. Now go.”
Adley let go of his wrist and the child half-ran and half-waddled through the crowds and disappeared. Adley opened the pouch and counted the coppers and silvers; there was more in his hand than most traders earned in a month – the boy was a master at his art. He didn’t know what to do with the money so he placed it in his pocket; perhaps they would be around during the next almsgiving. With that resolution, he smiled and looked for the bloodhound.
Sheiva was on the track, his nose close to the ground as he moved through the streets, pausing now and then to sniff at the air. Adley jogged to catch up, and after a short time they came to a halt outside a shop.
The dog moved close to Adley and muttered in a low growl, “She’s been here, I’m sure, though the track was confusing. I sensed her in many places, but the track here was strongest.”
Adley nodded and straightened his clothes. He knocked on the door.
So Chaeli had visited a healer . . . for the cut to her leg no doubt.
The door swung open and, after a moments hesitation, Adley stepped inside the shop. “Hello?”
He saw a bench tipped on one side and when he stepped further into the room, he heard a crunch under his feet; littering the floor were smashed bottles and apothecary jars, herbs and liquids spilling from the remnants. The mixture of medicines gave rise to a multitude of scents and fumes swirling in the air. It made him feel sick. A whine came from the door; Sheiva held up a bleeding paw.
“Stood on glass,” he whimpered.
“Stay there, let me check the place,” Adley whispered, drawing his spine sword. Nothing had been left untouched. He moved up the staircase and observed the sleeping area: the bed had been pulled out, the small table and chairs overturned. He checked the stove; it was still warm. Whatever had happened here was recent. Although there were clear signs of a struggle, he could read no signs to tell him more, and no sign to tell him if Chaeli had been involved. He went back downstairs. The dog had obeyed and stood in the doorway, paw still in the air. Adley shook his head and shrugged his shoulders at Sheiva.
“Try over there,” said Sheiva with a nod.
Adley saw a pile of papers scattered by the bench. He scanned them and identified the seal of a medic in the top corner; it was a letter to the King’s treasury requesting money to train an apprentice. It was signed Anya DeVaine.
“Adders, can you come and help?”
Adley left the papers and took hold of the injured paw. “No glass, just a small cut.” He patted Sheiva on the head. “You’ll live.”
The dog gingerly put his leg down. “What now Adders?”
Adley frowned. What now indeed? Where had she gone this time?
“I can track further if you like?”
“What else can we do? I have no idea what happened here, and I don’t think we can spare the time to wait and see if the medic returns.” Adley gave the room a final sweeping glance. “This was a quick extraction by several men, they were searching for something . . . or someone.”
“Do you think they have Chaeli?”
Adley shook his head. He looked out the shop doorway and into the streets, where soldiers still paced the walkways, identifying and harassing all the single women they could see.
“No, I think she’s still out here somewhere.”
The dog limped outside. He wagged his tail, signalling to Adley it was safe to follow. Sheathing his sword, Adley stepped out of the shop and closed the door. The dog wandered through the streets and back towards the gates.
Adley frowned. She had left the city? Why?
Chapter Four
Chaeli, and the man who called himself Danven, made their way across the wasteland towards the royal road. As the sun rose, Chaeli could make out the silhouettes of several large caravans.
“My girls,” Danven said, beaming in delight, his large face full of love and pride.
The walk had been brisk, and conversation between them flowed easily. Danven asked few questions of her, concentrating more on describing his troupe and their dancing. According to him, it seemed that the troupe had been to all the corners of Ibea and been an outstanding and unequalled success everywhere.
Danven clapped his hands as they drew closer to the caravans. All the doors opened and the three most exquisite women Chaeli had ever seen emerged, each accompanied by a broad, well-built man.
“Chaeli, these are my girls, my diamonds of the sky, my beautiful blooms!” Danven exclaimed.
One by one the three approached and kissed his cheeks. One had skin the colour of ebony, and her body shone with a glorious dark glow, her great almond-shaped eyes and high cheekbones giving her a regal appearance. Her jet hair was shorn close to her scalp, making her appearance even more striking. She kissed Chaeli on the forehead and murmured a greeting. Her voice sang to Chaeli’s blood, and intoxicated her.
The next woman seemed more beautiful than the first. Hair the colour of the sunset fell in a silken sheet across her creamy skin; she had violet eyes shaded by lashes so long that Chaeli wished to touch them to make sure they were real. Far smaller than Chaeli, she reached up and touched her face, then grasped her forearms in the traditional Porton greeting.
Finally, the most alluring of the three approached. Though only a little shorter than Chaeli, her body made Chaeli feel inadequate and bulbous. Her breasts were high, her legs long, and her skin graced with inkings from Sheema. It was the first time Chaeli had seen one of the mountain warrior women, and she stared in delight at the complex and colourful tattoos covering her arms and legs. Her hair was cut into a design of golden snakes, the inkings weaving their way over her neck and into the curls. She smiled and Chaeli thought she had fallen in love. It embarrassed her; this rush of emotion seemed as unnatural as it was irresistible. With a gentle kiss to Chaeli’s lips, the warrior stared deep, with eyes so dark as to be almost black, her lips so soft and warm that Chaeli almost lost her senses. She felt ugly in their presence, and their beauty belittled and silenced her.
Danven spoke in hushed tones to the men, keeping his elaborate gestures to a minimum. The men said nothing and nodded their acknowledgements; they wore the markings of slaves on their forearms. Chaeli stood at the edge of the group feeling awkward. She shivered; despite the rising sun she still felt so cold. The women laughed and she was filled with inexplicable longing.
One of the men approached and indicated a caravan. He said nothing and Chaeli climbed in without thought. Inside, sumptuous cushions were strewn across the floor, the walls were panelled with silk, and all about, beautiful and exotic colours seduced her senses. The three women followed her in.
“Chaeli. Such a beautiful name,” the warrior purred as she lay back on the cushions.
“For a special daughter,” added the redhead, walking to Chaeli and touching her dark hair.
The third smiled. “Come, join us, Chaeli. We shall be friends, yes?”
The caravan was intoxicating, the air thick with their perfume and the scent of their bodies. In some part of her, a memory – a thought of some kind – nagged at her, but she couldn’t remember what it was.
“Yes, we shall be friends.” She nodded and smiled. She sat back and relaxed, relishing the comfort. The women laughed in unison, their voices like distant temple bells.
Danven watched his girls surrounding Chaeli like bees on a flower. They made him so proud. He smiled to himself. The Damnable Lord would be pleased. He closed the doors to the caravan and placed a rune on the lock. Chaeli would soon forget everything and, once that happened, she could be fashioned into anything the Dark Prince wished.
*
Within an hour of leaving Anya, Nathan had tracked all his men down and explained the situation to them. It was agreed they would stay in the city and await his return. Adyam was the most resistant; he was eager and wanted to accompany Nathan everywhere. Though uncomfortable with the way the boy idolised him, for he had done nothing to deserve such adoration, Nathan found himself unable to quash his enthusiasm. He convinced the young man to stay, and gave him orders to join the King’s Guard, to be his eyes and ears in the elite section. Adyam had accepted the mission with gusto; it was only afterwards Nathan realised he had unwittingly increased the younger man’s devotion.
Satisfied that they would all follow orders, he made his way to the nearest gate. He couldn’t scale the wall now daybreak had arrived, there were too many civilians around, and too many guards. Weighing the coin in his pocket, he approached the guard.
Four silver pieces it cost him. Four silver pieces he handed to a weasel he could have flattened with one hand, and who, just a few years ago, would have quailed at the mere mention of the name DeVaine. He was furious, but was in no position to negotiate.
Making his way through the Sink was simple enough. Once out of view of the gate, he weaved and bobbed his way through the crowds with ease. He still had skills from the Underworld; his past as an assassin equipped him with the arts of concealment and disguise, while his life as a renegade had taught him to blend in amongst others. He could feel the sweet intoxication of his gifts and hear the call of the Underworld once more, but he pushed it to one side and concentrated on leaving. As the dwellings thinned, he searched the horizon hoping to see Chaeli near the road to Lindor; but there was nothing.
As he strode across the wasteland, caravans to the east caught his eye; they moved along the royal road to Lindor in steady convoy and he could just make out the name Danven in ornate lettering on each caravan.
Nathan sneered. He remembered this troupe: prostitutes in the guise of exotic courtesans and performers, the dancers whored out to rich men paying extortionate amounts of coin for one night of pleasure. He didn’t despise the art of whoring itself, but the way these women were used to gain political status and were offered as gifts and prizes sickened him.
Where was she? He had left her almost three hours ago; he’d spent too long with Adyam. She could be a few kilometres ahead by now and he couldn’t be sure she would follow the road. He gazed at the horizon: the road to Lindor was straight and stretched out as far as he could see, lined with hundreds of Trithian lehai trees. Their canopies shielded the road from the sun, allowing only bolts of light to stream through in golden bars. He re-arranged his pack and set off along the road. The caravans, smooth and well-maintained, were fast, and by jogging he managed to keep pace behind them. If they stopped, he would see if he could perhaps groom and feed the horses to earn a seat upfront with one of the drivers.
After an hour, Nathan was breathing heavily and a sheen of sweat covered his brow. He had kept a good pace, but knew he was tiring. There was still no sign of Chaeli; unless she was running, he should have caught her by now.
The caravans had started to pull away, appearing smaller as they moved ahead. He slowed to a walk, knowing he would need to conserve energy. As the sun bounced off the branches overhead and onto the ground, the light danced off the last droplets of dew. The world around him seemed so at peace, yet he found himself considering places for ambush and concealment. Swinging his pack around to his side, he removed an oat treat and smiled; Anya had packed his favourite, cooked oats rolled in honey and sprinkled with aritha vanilla. Chewing and savouring each mouthful, he considered his situation. He had rations for four days. The next village in this direction was six days, possibly five if he pushed hard. Could she have walked this fast? He thought back to their parting; she had been angry with him, to his regret – he hated the way his temper always seemed to get the better of him. Years of trying and he still couldn’t control the rage inside. Pathetic. Perhaps she’d decided to return home, or move on to another city. Nathan hesitated, then sank to his knees.
“Praise be the Eternal Kingdom,” he prayed, feeling as uncomfortable as always when asking the gods for help. “Divine Ones, I ask for your blessing and support. Please help me, I need to find her, I need to make sure she’s well.”
Nothing. Emptiness consumed him. Familiar feelings of pain, loneliness, and rage began seeping into him, spreading, filling the void. The rage was a thousand times stronger than any other emotion. Nathan recognised the signs that the Underworld wanted him to return, and despair wailed deep inside.
Not now, please, not now. He didn’t have the strength, nor clarity of mind, nor purity of heart to fight the Underworld. He closed his eyes and felt the serpents moving on his back, and as the furious torrent of darkness began to overwhelm him, it was as if a gust of ice cold wind stilled the serpents and froze him from the inside out. Then unfamiliar warmth exploded within him. Every inch of him burned, uncontrollable love and happiness dousing the rage, but then there was silence and nothingness once more.
He tried to stand, but staggered and fell back to his knees, as though forced to return to prayer. It was then he heard the voice on the wind: “For her.” There was a pause, and then: “Lindor.”
He didn’t have the strength to hunt for the source of the voice, and deep down he knew he wouldn’t find anyone nearby. Nathan had heard the accounts of those who were touched by the gods. Indeed, his own sister had tried often to describe her experience, but every time Nathan had silenced her, from disbelief and pain.
He knew the truth: the gods were watching and they did listen. But somehow it didn’t help. He felt worse, for he’d prayed to the Kingdom and begged for redemption, but received nothing. Today the gods had listened to his pleas – not for him, but for another.
He stood, his legs like jelly and his breathing ragged. His back burned as the serpents returned to their resting place and, re-arranging the pack, he walked on.
*
Chaeli struggled to keep her eyes open. Where was she again? She made herself sit up and look around. Three beautiful women lay next to her, their eyes closed. One look at them and Chaeli was transfixed – who were they? A rocking motion disoriented her for a moment before she realised she was in some kind of wagon with colourful silks covering the wooden walls and floor. As though peering through fog, she remembered meeting a strange man and being introduced to these women. The memory didn’t reassure her and she began to panic. What was going on? Why couldn’t she remember anything? The stale air of the caravan was beginning to make her feel sick; the mix of the perfumes and sweat from the women was too much for her to bear. She looked around, seeking a way out, or at least the chance to open a window. It was then that Chaeli was distracted by her clothing. She was wearing billowing, almost transparent yellow trousers. She ran her hands along the material, marvelling at the silk but then frowned, where were her clothes? Her breasts were covered by knotted cream silk wound around her and her stomach was bare. Her mouth parted and her cheeks burned, pulling her hair away from her neck she tried to cool herself down. It was too hot. Far too hot. Involuntarily, she covered her chest with her hands, even though there was no one to see her. This wasn’t right. Not right at all.
Something niggled at her, some memory. Something important she had to do. She tried to focus but the fog remained.
“Chaeli,” purred one of the women, opening her eyes. “Come here.” She held out her tattooed arms and Chaeli wanted to do nothing more than sink into them and sleep. The pull proved irresistible; she moved into the woman’s arms and lay down. The woman gently stroked her hair, and Chaeli fell back into a deep dreamless sleep.
When she woke, they had stopped moving. Shivering, she rubbed her arms as the women stood up and the rear of the caravan opened. She squinted as the sun streamed through the open doors. Chaeli realised the figure at the doors was the strange man that she hazily remembered.
“Where am I?”
The man smiled and stroked his clipped beard. “My rose, you are with Danven. Come.”
Chaeli felt no fear, and clambered to her feet, walking into his open arms.
He held her close to his chest. “Chaeli, so pretty. My rose, Danven has a gift for you.”
That niggling memory was back: what was she supposed to be doing? Chaeli fought to bring it into focus. As the man led her to another caravan, she noticed several man-slaves milling around. One had started a fire, another cooked, while the third stood with his back to Chaeli. Danven indicated a highly polished trunk, so large it could accommodate a full grown man. The deep chestnut colour gleamed and the golden hinges shone. Chaeli gasped as the slave lifted the lid: nestling on pillows of silk and satin cascaded gems every imaginable colour, shape, and size. Firestones from Mischla twinkled up at her, the yellow and red veins of colour in the crystal so unique and rare that she gasped in shock. Darting her eyes around the glittering treasure she restrained herself from touching.
“You may choose anything you wish,” purred Danven, placing his hand on her back and stroking the exposed skin.
Chaeli ran her fingers through rings, necklaces, bracelets, and anklets, dazzled by the glinting of silver and gold, of diamonds, rubies, and gems unknown to her, mounted in blue, grey, and black crystal.
“Anything?” she asked in awe.
“Anything,” he replied still tracing shapes on her back.
She continued to stare as though hypnotised or enchanted; a slave brought a padded stool and an oval mirror. Standing in silence, he held the latter whilst she admired herself in one piece after another. She held up a black crystal necklace, but it was too weighty. Sighing in ecstasy, she slipped on a diamond and tourmaline ring. Light danced off the complex network of facets, and it sparkled like the rainbow; yet, though glorious and enthralling, it didn’t feel right on her finger. She wanted something to make her feel special, to make her feel as beautiful as the women. She was so absorbed that she paid no attention to the setting sun, the rising moon, didn’t notice the light changing to a silvery hue, nor the drop in temperature.
Something had caught her eye: a small choker. Lifting it out, she felt a jolt of energy. She had to have it – it wanted her, and she it.
“This one,” she murmured.
“Let me see, my rose.” He held out his hand and examined the choker, a small silver band with a teardrop sapphire mounted in silver with small emeralds surrounding it like a nest.
“Simply wonderful,” he declared. “May I?” He indicated her neck and she lifted her hair. Smiling to himself, he placed the necklace around her and the sapphire shone in the moonlight. Chaeli admired the necklace in the mirror. It reminded her of something, or someone, but she couldn’t remember what, and felt her efforts dissolving as the silver warmed on her skin. One of the women arrived to lead her back to the caravan.
“You look tired. You must sleep,” she declared. Chaeli nodded and, crawling onto the cushions, she closed her eyes once more. The touch of the silk and the warmth of the necklace cradled her to sleep.
The woman closed the back of the caravan and joined the others by the campfire. “She sleeps, Master.” She bowed her head to Danven.
“Good. By morning she will have forgotten everything.” He sat on a large cushion by the fire, the other two women by his feet. “Are you hungry, my children?” he cooed.
“Yes, Master, so hungry,” all three women chorused.
Danven glanced at the slaves who stood behind him staring into the distance. They shook, though not with cold.
“Take them, my children, and feed. But not too much.”
The women laughed in delight and clapped their hands, each selecting a man and guiding him to a caravan with unnatural speed. Danven heard the succubi laugh as they closed the caravan doors behind them. He laughed to himself, too. Such beauties, his girls, such good children. Whilst they fed and Chaeli slept, Danven stayed alert, watching and listening around the camp. He was so close to earning a favour from his prince, so close to the ultimate reward.
*
Adley and Sheiva had tracked Chaeli to the road to Lindor, but her scent had vanished, as though she had disappeared. Sheiva sniffed the ground numerous times but there was nothing.
“Adders, I’m sorry.” His tail was low and his ears down.
The protector rubbed the dog’s head. “Don’t worry, Shiv. We’ll head this way.” He sensed a warm rush and knew that Elek was guiding him; he had often thought that Elek indulged the mortal plane too much, but today he was grateful.
They walked all day. They had travelled without real rest for almost three days, and now they decided they had to make camp and refresh themselves. As the sun set, Sheiva bounded into the woods and back to the road several times, bringing sticks for a fire, while Adley arranged and lit them with tinder from his pack. A brilliant puff of light brightened the sky and there was a soft meow.
“Good to see you back to your preferred self, Shiv,” said Adley with an indulgent smile as he unwrapped some dried meat from his pack.
The cat purred and rubbed his head on Adley’s arm. “I’m so hungry, Adders.”
The protector handed him a slice of meat. “Got to pace ourselves. We don’t have many rations.” The cat gobbled the meat in two mouthfuls and gave a low growl of contentment and understanding. His small silver-white form curled around Adley’s pack and he closed his eyes.
Adley stared at the fire, chewing at the tough jerky. Elek had shown him they were on the right track, but where was she? He swallowed over the lump in his throat that had little to do with the meat.
*
Danven glanced along the road in the direction of Trithia. The sky had been illuminated by a bright flash. He narrowed his eyes and frowned.
*
Nathan lay staring at the stars. He hadn’t wanted a fire this evening, he wanted nothing but harsh solitude. The sky was dark, almost black, and the clear cloudless expanse revealed the constellations. A white flash filled the sky, but when Nathan blinked, it had gone.
*
“Remember,” whispered the voice. Chaeli sat bolt upright, her memories flooding back. Nathan, Anya, the guards, Danven, the choker. Instinctively she touched her neck and fondled the silver band; it was hot to the touch. Danven had tricked her! She scrambled up and over the cushions to the rear of the caravan, and pulled on the handle. It wouldn’t open. Cursing, she pulled the latch down again, this time putting more weight on the door by leaning against it with her shoulder, but it still wouldn’t budge.
Chaeli looked around the caravan, but could see nothing to aid her escape. Hurling cushions to the walls, she searched, looking for a weapon, a trapdoor, something. She pulled at the silks on the walls, but there were no windows. She was trapped in a wooden box.
She sat down on the cushions, and with each passing minute her memories returned sharper and clearer. Danven was not to be trusted. He had tricked her and seduced her into joining him and his troupe of . . . well, she wasn’t sure what they were a troupe of, but she knew it wasn’t something she wanted to join.
As she sat there contemplating her situation, she heard a bolt being drawn across the door. Forcing herself not to panic, she lay down and closed her eyes. The door opened with a creak and Chaeli blinked and stretched.
“Where am I?” she said, rubbing her eyes and peering at the door where Danven stood beaming at her. He was dressed in even more garish silks that stretched over his enormous stomach.
“Chaeli, my rose, you are with your protector.” He beckoned. His thick fingers revolted her as they curled into a cupping motion with a kind of obscene delicacy.
Chaeli kept her face a mask. She wanted to scream and run, but knew that she couldn’t show fear else this man would know she was no longer under his power. He helped her out of the caravan. The sun was just rising; she had lost a whole day and now unwanted thoughts of Nathan entered her mind. He would think she had left him, failing to keep to their agreement.
But what does that matter?
She hadn’t been sure he would even come for her. Why would he? He had Anya, the Games, his men. What was Chaeli to him? She was alone and needed to keep reminding herself of that.
She forced a smile. “I need to freshen up.”
“Of course.” He nodded to one of the silent slaves. “Take Chaeli to my caravan. Stay with her.” The slave nodded his acknowledgement and indicated for Chaeli to follow him. She cursed under her breath. She had hoped she could use the opportunity to run, but Danven was not taking any chances.
The slave guided her to the caravan. Pale and drawn, his skin was wrinkled and didn’t seem to fit his frame, as though he had wasted away during the night. His face was expressionless, his eyes half-closed as he followed her into the caravan. His proximity made her uncomfortable, but the physical distaste seemed the least of her worries. He pointed to the wash basin and chamber pot and moved to the corner, standing with his head bowed.
Chaeli couldn’t use the pot while being watched, so washed her face instead.
“Danven is a marvellous man,” she said, trying to provoke a response in the hope of getting friendliness or, at least, information in return, but he stood motionless and unresponsive. She stared at him as she patted her face dry. “The women are very beautiful.” The slave shifted and clenched his fists. Something told her to risk going further – it was the first reaction she had seen outside Danven’s control, and she had nothing to lose.
“Help me,” she begged, dropping to the floor and grasping the slave’s hands. “For the love of the gods, get me out of this place!”
He glanced at the door and back to her.
“Please!” Hysteria jangled in her voice.
He held a finger to his mouth “Shhhh,” he hissed. “I can’t help you, I can’t defy the demon.” His voice was deep and low, with a foreign lilt.
“Please, I need to get away from here, I don’t know what’s going on but it’s not . . . I have to leave!”
“You do not know?” asked the slave in wonder. He pulled her to her feet and checked the door of the caravan to ensure they were not overheard. “Danven is bad, very bad. Those women are his creatures.” He closed his eyes and Chaeli saw the remaining colour drain from his face as his body began to shake with violent spasms.
“I have to leave,” she repeated. “I don’t know what they want with me.”
The servant opened his eyes, still shaking. “You are the daughter, the one,” he whispered. “He wants you.”
“Well he’s got me!” She grabbed his arms again. “I need your help, I can’t do this without you, please. Please help me.”
“No, you do not understand! Danven wants you, yes, but not for himself. For his master.” He pulled away from Chaeli and moved back towards the door. “I have said too much. Hurry, Danven will be coming soon.”
“Are you going to help me?” she asked again. There was a long silence as they stared at one another.
At last the slave shook his head, as though clearing his mind. “You can’t escape him, no matter how hard you try to – he will find you.” The slave slumped and stared at the floor. “Once caught, you are never free.”
Chaeli touched his arm and he looked at fingers and then her face. Swallowing, she ran her fingers up his arm. “Please help me, I would be very grateful.” She licked her lips nervously and smiled at the slave, mimicking the women see had seen in the Sink.
“What are you doing?” He pulled away from her again and backed into the caravan shaking his head in disgust. As he pushed past, she stumbled and fell to the floor, landing on her hands and knees with a thud. She heard the slave sigh and he reached down, holding out his hand.
“I’m sorry,” she murmured, as she took his proffered help and stood. “I thought . . . I thought you might . . .” Humiliation burned in her cheeks.
“I wear the mark of a slave and have lost my freedom, but I have not lost my honour. You are a child in my eyes and I would never touch a child.”
She hung her head in shame, then her shame twisted into something akin to spite and she blurted, “You have honour, but you won’t help a child?”
“What can you know of honour? I am Kia Firestorm Jeherez Sar, son of the great Maji chieftain, Sar Sunspear Jeherez Nol, leader of the Northwind tribe of the Mischlan people. The greatest of the thirteen tribes.” Kia stood upright and Chaeli stepped back. At full height he was intimidating.
“Why are you here then?”
“I was captured by my father’s greatest enemy, the Longplain tribe. They beat and drugged me and sold me to this…demon. I tried to escape many times during the first year, but always Danven hunted me down. He gave me to his creatures and . . .” His voice became a whisper and she struggled to hear him. “Soon I did not wish to escape, soon I longed for their touch.” He raised his hands to his face and Chaeli heard soft sobs, saw his body shake. She reached out to him again and stroked his arm, this time in an attempt to comfort, but still he shook her touch away. “I was once a great warrior and now . . .”
“Will you help me? Please, I have no-one else. Come with me, we can leave together.”
Kia gazed into her eyes. “I was your age when I was captured. I have been his slave for ten years.” Chaeli looked up at him in surprise at the heavy lines around his face, his sagging skin, his jowls and his wrinkled hands. He saw her expression, and touched his face. “The women,” he said by way of explanation. Then he nodded. “I will try to help you, and reclaim some of the honour of the Northwind tribe. But you must be patient. I can do nothing now. Please, do not speak to me again, and do not look at me.” Kia stared down at the floor.
Chaeli wanted to scream, but instead she sighed. She couldn’t face the thought of relieving herself in front of a man. Outside, the fresh air taunted her and she knew she was going to be locked in that windowless box again. Chaeli ensured her mask was smooth as she looked at Danven. He had surrounded himself with the three women whilst the slaves packed up their camp.
“My rose!” he cried, rushing past the women towards her. She felt nothing but loathing for his form, for his gaudy clothes, his wet lips and drooping eyes; he was sweating in the early morning sun and dark blotches disfigured his silks. As he came closer she fought the urge to strike him and instead made herself smile.
“You look tired,” he said, concern distorting his face and somehow rendering it even more unpleasant. “You should sleep.”
“Yes, I am sleepy.”
“Ladies, help Chaeli to the caravan, she needs more sleep.”
The women surrounded her, two taking her arms and the third following closely behind. Chaeli hated their touch, but there was no way she could break free – their grip was gentle but unyielding. They locked her back in the caravan and she lay alone staring at the ceiling. The horses pulled away and she knew they were on the road again. Where did they intend to take her? Who was Danven’s master? She closed her eyes and prayed.
“My gods, please help me! I don’t know what’s going on, I need you, I need help.” She repeated her prayer over and over again until it became a mantra.
No warmth filled her. Who was it that had visited her earlier, clearing the fog and letting her memories return? Chaeli closed her eyes and thought of Acelle. She had always taken care of her; when she was bullied by other children, she had fought for her; when seven-year-old Chaeli had fallen from a tree, she had been the one to carry her to the medic, refusing to rest or leave her side until the healer had proclaimed her fit and chided Acelle for wasting his time. She laughed at her stupid jokes, and when their parents had died, she had been there, always guiding her. She was the perfect big sister.
They weren’t your parents though, were they? whispered the treacherous voice in her head. All her memories of Glin and Nina were full of warmth and light; they had shown her nothing but kindness and love. Yes, they were her parents.
She touched the choker. The sapphire was cool to the touch but the silver was warm . . . and then the memory of how she obtained the jewellery rose up to disgust her. She reached behind and tried to remove it, but the clasp wouldn’t undo and she couldn’t feel a join. She yanked harder at the clasp, but it would not budge.
*
Nathan couldn’t sleep that night and it seemed a waste of time even trying; he stood and stretched his legs and prepared to move again. Fastening his pack on his back, he set off along the road to Lindor, the memory of that unnatural flash of light playing on his mind.
As the sun rose, he saw dark shadows in the distance. His training took over and he moved from the road to the cover of the trees. There, he made out the hulking shapes of the caravans; he counted three men and three women. Then another man, a broad figure dressed in silks and covered in jewellery, left the largest caravan.
Nathan gripped the hilt of his dagger until his knuckles turned white. His vision blurred and a bitter taste of copper filled his mouth before a rush of anger and bloodlust hit him: he knew he taste of the Underworld too well to ignore warning signs. The serpents on his back stirred and began to writhe across his skin. It took all his concentration to still them and keep his fear at bay. The silk-clad man opened one of the caravans and a figure emerged, dazed and stumbling: Chaeli! Nathan almost shouted to her in relief.
By the gods, what is she wearing?
Her legs were covered in a thin layer of diaphanous silk – the harem of Velenese Barons wore such clothes, for despite the cold and wet climate in their country, they kept their women scantily clad like those from the Mischlan tribes. Her breasts were covered only by one strip of darker silk knotted behind her back. He was momentarily mesmerised by her body, so pale with skin like cream; he pictured her in his arms. Then, with no small effort, he pushed the thought out of his mind, not knowing where it came from nor why.
“Take Chaeli to my caravan, stay with her,” the man said, tone dripping with false cordiality. A slave – he was sure now of their status from the way they held themselves – led Chaeli into another caravan. As Nathan drew nearer, the reek of the Underworld smothered him and made his eyes sting.
He waited at the tree-line for Chaeli to emerge; when she did, she went straight to the heavy man who then locked her in the first caravan. What was she doing? The three women went into their own caravan, the man into his, leaving the slaves to feed and water the horses. Nathan crept forward and tried to open the rear door of Chaeli’s caravan but it wouldn’t move. He studied the simple metal lock and realised it had been sealed with a holding rune; there was no way he could get through it without the man knowing – as soon as he broke the charm, the conjurer would be alerted.
He moved back out of sight while the slaves started the convoy moving. He jogged alongside under cover of the greenery, and once he knew the slaves were focused on the road, he silently darted out and grabbed the back of the last caravan. He lifted his feet on to it, hanging on the footstep as they moved, and thanked the gods for the small mercy that Chaeli’s was at the rear. After a few minutes his arms and fingers began to ache, so he swung down and looked beneath the caravan: solid. Cursing, he crawled back. He would have to break the rune and hope he could get Chaeli out of there before all hell broke loose – perhaps literally, judging by the Underworld stench. He held on with his right hand and placed his left over the rune. Drawing from his energy and reciting the charm, the inking on his palm shifted, the seven stars moved into a circle and enclosed the rune. His hand burned, but he couldn’t stop; slowly, the rune began to dissolve.
“Faster, damn you, faster!” He moaned and drew on more of his energy, jeopardising his grip on the caravan to push through the magical ward quicker. With a final, agonising flash of heat through his hand, the rune vanished. He breathed a sigh of relief and pulled on the door handle, but as he did so, all three caravans stopped and he was jerked forward. He had been detected. With no time to waste, he flung the door open to find Chaeli lying on a profusion of cushions, her eyes closed.
“Chaeli!”
Her eyes snapped open and the comprehension dawned. “Nathan!” she cried, scrambling to her feet to run into his waiting arms.
“We have to leave. Now!” He jumped to the ground, Chaeli right behind him.
“Can you run?” he asked. She nodded, and he grabbed her hand. Shivers danced over her skin at his touch, though she couldn’t tell their nature. In any case, she had no time for that now; the three slaves surrounded them.
Chaeli panicked. Not now, not Nathan! What would Danven do to him? She stared at Kia who looked at Nathan with pity. Chaeli knew that Danven wouldn’t allow him to live, but she couldn’t let him die because of her. Nathan let go of her hand and drew his sword, but the slaves stood firm.
“Move,” roared Nathan channelling the energy into his voice to make it wild and commanding.
“My rose,” called Danven, “what is this?” He approached and the line of slaves parted for him.
“She’s not your anything,” growled Nathan, taking Chaeli’s arm and pulling her behind him.
Gods, the smell of him, the scent of his power!
Danven smiled and Chaeli saw sharp pointed fangs. “She is mine. Come to me, Chaeli,” he commanded, crooking a finger at her.
Chaeli was rooted to the spot, her legs refusing to move and her mouth dry. Danven dominated her sight; all she could see when he spoke were flashes of the fangs. She shook her head, drawing strength from Nathan and his protection.
“Never,” she whispered, her voice broken and low. She cleared her throat and took a deep breath. “Never!”
Danven growled, and the three women appeared alongside the slaves. Chaeli and Nathan were trapped within the arc of enemies. Chaeli reached down and drew a curved blade from the sheath at Nathan’s side; she would die before Danven would claim ownership of her again.
“My master won’t allow you to be hurt, Chaeli. We won’t hurt you, come, leave the mortal, come to Danven and embrace your father. Come, embrace his love.”
“Not. A. Fucking. Chance.”
Danven roared and his form changed. He grimaced as his clothes split and dark red and black flesh swelled in lumps along his body. His hands twisted and snapped, and his bones cracked and crunched. Long black claws emerged, and he pointed the vicious talons at her.
“He will die, Chaeli. He will. Whether quickly or painfully, it is up to you.”
His claws brought memories of Malo to Chaeli’s mind, and suddenly she couldn’t breathe, her legs almost buckling under the weight of the unwanted terror that gripped her again.
Nathan put an arm out to steady her, never allowing his gaze to leave the arc. “Don’t, Chaeli,” he warned. “What happens to me doesn’t matter, his word is meaningless.”
Danven laughed. The booming sound bounced off the caravans making the birds from the nearby trees scatter. Then it was eerily quiet. The sound of splitting skin, and the stench of putrefying flesh filled the air. Two huge black wings unfolded from behind Danven. His face contorted, bone formed on his forehead and his eyes changed to fiery red.
“A morthon,” Nathan muttered, “I might have known.”
“Not just any morthon, my knowledgeable mortal. The morthon, father of all morthons, master of the incubi and succubi.”
The women too began to transform. Their beauty remained, but it was the beauty of deepest night, eyes black like coals, dark nails and deadly talons extending from their fingers. Wings emerged from their backs, too, and they hissed at Chaeli, venom spraying from their lips and hitting the ground, sizzling on impact. Chaeli glanced at Kia, who wrung his hands and darted his gaze between Danven, the succubi and Chaeli before giving her the slightest of nods.
One of the women lunged at Nathan, who feinted to the right and sliced at the creature’s wing, making contact with the membrane and spraying blood over her sisters. The creature howled in pain and scurried to the morthon, whimpering and hissing. She threw Chaeli a savage glare and bared her fangs.
“Careful,” warned Danven, “Master wants her intact, not a hair on her head is to be harmed. The mortal, however, will be yours, my darlings.”
Chaeli moved in front of Nathan, the dagger still drawn.
“What are you doing?”
“Trust me.” The confidence in her voice hid her uncertainty; she didn’t know where her strength was coming from. It wasn’t her gods. But at this moment, she didn’t have the time or inclination to question it. She pressed back, pushing Nathan against the caravan. “If you want him, you’ll have to come through me,” she declared.
Ye gods! What am I saying?
The creatures stared at their master. Danven moved closer, batting two of the slaves to the ground. Their bodies crumpled at his touch, and they dragged themselves out of range, trembling with pain, breaking the arc. Chaeli silently pleaded with Kia, but he stepped back. His face was contorted by fear.
“Do not defy your father’s wishes. He wants you and will destroy the whole of this world to get you.” Danven transformed back to his mortal form. “Come with me, join your father and you will be raised to greatness, to power. My lord will show you the wonders of the Underworld. You will be revered and adored by all.”
Chaeli stared at him. Lies, all of it lies. She pressed harder against Nathan. Despite the dangers, the sheer horror of the moment, she shivered when his hand came to rest on her waist. Danven stepped closer, almost within a swords reach. Chaeli shook all over; she had no game plan, and he would be within touching distance in a matter of seconds.
One of the creatures screamed and Danven turned at the sound. A black handled blade protruded from the squealing succubus’s chest. She collapsed to the ground, writhing in agony as the wound began to smoke. Curling into a ball, skin bubbling, green liquid slid from her eyes to pool in the dirt beside her. Another wrist-blade flew from the trees and hit a second creature in the chest; she screamed like the first and dropped to the road. Black smoke poured from both women, stinking of rot and decomposing flesh.
Danven turned back to Chaeli, his eyes wild and uncomprehending. His form began to turn again and he lunged towards her, claws bared. Nathan knocked her to the ground and raised his sword. Claws made contact with steel and Nathan struggled to hold firm as Danven lashed out. A talon struck Nathan’s forearm, drawing blood.
The remaining succubus flew forward to grab Chaeli by the throat with one claw and her arm with the other. Chaeli couldn’t move, and every time she tried to twist or turn, the talons pushed deeper into the flesh of her arm, the grip on her throat tightening.
“Kia, help me, please . . .” she gasped. The slave moved, but instead of stepping forward, he stepped back and further out of view.
A battle cry sounded from the trees behind and the creature turned to the noise. A flash of silver and the succubus’s head rolled from her shoulders, the limp talons finally releasing Chaeli from their grasp. She stumbled several feet away before looking up in disbelief; the man who had fought Malo rode towards the group on a silver horse.
“Come, Chaeli,” he called, reaching down. She shied away and looked back at Nathan, who was still fighting Danven. Without another thought, she picked up the dagger and ran towards the morthon. With a frustrated cry, she plunged the dagger into its back between the monstrous wings. The beast roared and reached around, his claws clutching the dagger and pulling it loose. He reared up and Chaeli fell to the ground, where he turned on her and roared. Nathan, taking advantage of this momentary distraction, sent a swift kick into the beast’s side, which allowed him to finally leap away from the caravan. Danven snarled and swiped for him, but Nathan ducked and plunged his sword under the beast’s left arm, up and into the heart.
The beast screamed in agony, black blood pouring from the wound. Nathan moved away as it fell to its knees. Curling his wings around his body, Danven fell silent in his cocoon.
Both Nathan and Adley ran to Chaeli, reaching her side together, each staring at other with suspicion. Chaeli groaned and sat up. She immediately turned to Nathan and grasped his arm.
“Nathan,” she whispered. He reached down, touched her face and smiled.
*
Adley stepped back and eyed the tall dark man with suspicion. He was sick to his stomach, his instinctive jealousy turning to anger; he was her protector, he alone. The way the dark man knelt by her side, stroking her hair so gently, galled him.
Nathan looked up and nodded. “Thank you.”
Adley nodded back. “I’m here for her,” he said.
Nathan narrowed his eyes and pulled Chaeli to her feet, standing protectively in front of her.
“No, you misunderstand, I am sent to protect her,” Adley explained, raising his hands.
“Protect me?” Chaeli mumbled. “I’ve seen you before, at my house. You fought that other demon.”
“I am Adley, your protector.” He beckoned the horse over. “This is Sheiva.” In a flash of light, the horse transformed into a large silver cat, who purred so loudly Chaeli could feel it in the ground beneath her.
“Ye gods,” she muttered, rubbing her head and staring at them.
Adley smiled and bent down to stroke the cat. “Not quite.”
“Adley. Good name,” said Nathan, sheathing his sword. “I’ve not heard that name since I was told the stories of the Kitaani and the Dragon Wars as a boy.”
Adley shrugged. “That all happened close to a thousand years ago, if I remember rightly.”
Nathan helped her to her feet and Chaeli approached Adley. He found he was holding his breath; he drank in the details, the curves and planes of her face. Her eyes were the colour of the sunlit ocean, dark hair fell like night down her back. She had a smattering of freckles over her nose. His heart sped up as she moved closer to inspect him. She took in the scars from his battle with Malo, and frowned, making him panic. Would she find him unattractive? Or worse, repellent? Would she not want him?
She doesn’t want you anyway, the whisper inside taunted.
She said nothing but turned to Nathan. “I need to find my clothes.”
Nathan threw Adley a quizzical glance, then left to search the caravans.
“Chaeli,” came a deep voice, startling both Adley and Chaeli. Kia edged forward, head bowed. “Your clothes were burned,” he said.
Chaeli scowled. “You said you’d help! You lied.”
He raised his head and tried to meet her gaze. Adley saw the pain in Kia’s eyes and took pity on the slave as Chaeli folded her arms and pouted like a child. Adley began to speak, to suggest that she needed to understand before condemning, but Kia spoke first.
“I am weak and unworthy, forgive me.” He turned on his heel and slunk away between the caravans and out of sight. A feeling of disquietude crept over Adley; there was something about the slave that didn’t sit right.
“My lady, if I may?” offered Adley, removing his jacket and handing it to her. She smiled nervously. His heart leapt like a spring lamb, and he grinned back.
Glancing after Kia, the feeling just wouldn’t leave him and, reluctantly, Adley left Chaeli to follow the slave. As he reached the rear of the caravan he saw Kia on his knees, a large Mischlan curved blade in his hand.
“Forgive me, Father.”
Realisation filled the protector and he lunged forward to grab the knife. He was too late. Kia Firestorm Jeherez Sar plunged the metal blade into his stomach and dragged it up to his sternum in one fluid, determined motion. His body slumped forward. Adley rolled the slave onto his back, blood pouring from the wound and into the dirt. Kia shook violently, his body desperately clinging to the last precious seconds. Tears formed in his eyes and he tried to speak, but instead of words, only rasps escaped. Adley whispered a prayer of sending as the slave died; it was habit more than anything else, for Kia would not be entering the Kingdom following his suicide. His spirit would now become part of the land. Adley pressed his fingertips to the slave’s eyelids and closed the staring eyes.
“Adders?” Sheiva’s mournful mew startled the protector. “What happened?”
“A tribute to the land. Atonement for the shame he brought his father.” They both stared at the prone body in silence. “I’ll bury him. Don’t let Chaeli follow.”
“That’s the cause of all this trouble?” asked Sheiva incredulously. “That girl?
Adley fought the urge to kick the cat, instead saying through his teeth, “She’s not just any girl.”
“I know that, Adders, she’s the daughter of the Underworld. I would never underestimate her.”
“She’s nothing like Eli,” snapped Adley. “She’s kind, gentle, naïve, beautiful—”
“Beautiful?” The cat stared up at him and meowed. “Beautiful? Are you quite sure Adders? All I see is trouble and disaster. Are you in love with her?”
The protector stared at the cat. “I am a protector. Love is forbidden to me,” he recited.
The cat held his gaze. “That’s not an answer.”
Adley sighed deeply. “I am a protector. To love is forbidden. I know my place.” He knelt by the fallen former slave and son of the Northwind Tribe’s chieftain and began to perform the burial rites.
The cat rubbed his head against Adley’s side.
*
Dal fingered the ring in his pocket. He had been carrying it now for several days and was still no closer to finding out the identity of the marketplace woman. He had sent several watchers into the city with detailed descriptions of her, but they returned with nothing. He set about searching for some trace in the city records, but the only Dresne family he could find were a couple of traders who packed up and left Trithia years ago, their only child a daughter named Acelle.
So where had she come from? He considered she might have given him a false name, but dismissed the thought as quickly as it came, for Dal knew the look of a liar. He needed to find her, to apologise for his behaviour and any embarrassment he might have caused. In truth, he wanted to see her again, see whether those eyes were the way he remembered, whether her smile was as dazzling as he recalled, or whether his entire infatuation was the result of fermented grapes and spices. As his mood worsened, his brother arrived with a molyona card deck.
“Dal, where have you been? I missed you at the supper last night. And you missed all the fun. The captain actually smiled, I promise you!” Fyn paced his apartments, using his hands to emphasise everything he said, and Dal feared that nothing would silence him today.
“Are you sure he didn’t just have wind, brother?”
“What’s wrong with you?”
“I’ve been searching for someone, and she has eluded me,” he replied flatly.
“Not you as well? The captain has the entire King’s Guard looking for Nathan DeVaine and some woman accompanying him. Apparently Stirm is going wild at the thought of DeVaine entering his city. Still, I have to say, it’s pretty bold.”
Fyn had Dal’s full attention now. “A young woman? What does she look like?”
The younger man shrugged. “Tall, dark hair, blue eyes. Name’s Chandy or Kaylee or something like that.”
“Chaeli,” Dal murmured. Why did the King want this woman? His woman.
His brother stopped shuffling the molyona deck and regarded Dal with suspicion. “Yes, that’s it! How did you— Wait a moment! Is she the one you’re looking for? Why? And what does Father want with her?”
“It’s not for Father, it’s for me.” Dal held up his hand to forestall more questions. “Tell me what you know of this girl, Fyn, and please, leave nothing out.”
“All right, all right. I don’t know much, just what I heard last night. Apparently she accompanied DeVaine into the city over a week ago. No one knew they’d arrived until the guard at the west gate had one too many in a tavern and blabbed. DeVaine paid him a healthy purse to remain silent, but he’s none too healthy for it now. When Stirm found out, he had guards searching everywhere for DeVaine. Started with his sister’s place, but the watchers saw no sign of man or girl. Actually, they didn’t even know about the girl until a few days later when the King summoned the entire guard to the throne room.” Fyn leaned forward. “Stirm was excited! He promised a shower of gold and a huge promotion to the soldier who brings in DeVaine.” Fyn paused for dramatic effect. “But you’d expect that, wouldn’t you?” He paused again, then hurried on when he saw the look in Dal’s eyes. “Anyway, the real talking point was that he promised a knighthood to the one who brought him this girl.”
Dal slammed his hands on the table. “And you didn’t think to come and give your big brother this information?”
“Well, no, sorry, I didn’t.” The younger man deflated, his hands stilled on the card deck.
“Sorry, Fyn. I’m sorry, it’s not your fault.” Dal hesitated, then decided to throw caution to the wind. “I met her in the market a few days ago and I haven’t been able to stop thinking about her, it’s as though I’m enchanted. . .”
“By sweet Penella, Dal, you, lord of the bachelors, the royal singleton, have been snared by a woman in the market?” Fyn doubled over in laughter while Dal grinned wryly. “So then, brother, who is she and why does the King want her?”
“That’s just it, Fyn, I don’t know. I can find no record of her in the city archives. But I’ll tell you one thing, I was captivated at first glance, and if I’d been in her thrall any longer I would have thrown away everything for the chance to be with her.”
“Strong words.”
“She raises such strong emotions in me, I can’t explain it. Never have I felt this way. So, is the guard any closer to finding her?”
“No, no sign of them. We’ve taken DeVaine’s sister into custody and she’s in the dungeons awaiting interrogation. She’ll crack soon, no doubt.”
“If she does, let me know, brother. I must find . . . Chaeli.” His pleasure in saying her name was marred by the knowing expression on Fyn’s face.
“Of course,” his brother. “Now, are we going to play molyona or are you going to pine for this seductress all night?”
Dal laughed, feeling it better to conceal how little he felt like humour. He nodded. “Get on with it, Fyn. I long to relieve you of your purse.”
*
Chaeli found Nathan in the morthon’s caravan. He had been searching through boxes and chests; parchments, paper and vellum covered the floor while he sat reading.
“Nathan?” she called from the doorway.
He looked up. “The morthon kept good records, very clear and detailed. He’s been using his succubi on the richest and most influential men in Ibea. The women fed on the men at night, the victims becoming addicted to their touch.” He stopped reading. “Chaeli you’re hurt.” He dropped the papers and rushed over, kneeling in front of her to examine her wounds. “They’re deep, but they’ll heal.” He looked up at her.
She gazed into his eyes and realised they were the same colour as her pendant. Involuntarily she touched her neck.
“She heals fast because of who she is,” stated Adley with cold certainty from behind her. He stood refastening the blades to his wrists; he didn’t look up.
“Who is she?”
Adley looked up now and scowled, the hostility clear. Nathan straightened his stance.
“So who is she?” he asked, making it less of a question and more of a demand. Chaeli turned to face Adley, confused by his statement and by the animosity all around her. Adley stood silent, his hand now on the hilt of his sword. At last he nodded. “She is the daughter of Prince Eli, Lord of the Underworld. Her mother was Amelia Von Ariseré, the mortal lover of Prince Daro.” He turned and walked away from the caravan and back to Sheiva’s side.
“Smooth, Adders, really smooth,” the cat muttered. “Been practising that in a mirror?”
Chaeli stumbled out of the entrance and fell. Nathan lifted her up and she pushed him away, running her hands through her hair. She needed to be alone. The demon had spoken the truth. The memory of its smell and touch made her shudder and she tried to concentrate on thoughts of her parents and Acelle but they faded in and out of her memory. She wasn’t her sister, they weren’t her parents. She wasn’t anyone. She needed to breathe, but now the choker felt tighter, too tight. She clawed at her throat. “Get this thing off me,” she gasped.
Nathan moved behind her and lifted her hair. “There’s no clasp. How did you get this on?”
“Of course there’s a clasp!” she snapped, grasping at the anger. But she couldn’t hold onto it, and began to feel light-headed. She closed her eyes as nausea washed over her.
“Chaeli, there isn’t a clasp. It’s a single silver band, I can’t remove it.”
She couldn’t breathe. The tightness of the choker and miserable resignation that everything she knew was a lie was trapped inside her wouldn’t let her take in air; her eyes closed and her body went limp
Chapter Five
“Mercy, Master! Great one, forgive me,” begged Danven.
The prince watched poison from the dagger dance into his morthon’s heart.
